“THE BULLETIN" BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to the price stated (which is
published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
MISS MILNE AND I, a story by Dr.
Mannington Caffyn (paper, 2s 6d;
cloth, 3s), has some interest, though the
plot is fantastic and a little foolish. But
many shrewd remarks are intersprinkled,
and the chapters on the Sydney smallpox
scare are very readable and especially
attractive to Australians. Dr. Caffyn
satirises mercilessly the stupidity and
cowardice of Government and people, and
the Quarantine Station at North Head
finds itself infamously famous. The pas-
sage in which Dr. Caffyn sums up his
opinions of Australia is Worth quoting :
I’m at the back of God’s speed, at the tail-end
of His earth, amid a strange people who are
Americans without the American’s “go,’’ and
English without the ballast of the Englishman’s
conservatism ; in a land to which have flocked the
venturesome and the visionary, the restless and
the discontented, the utopian dreamer and the
truant son, ail mixed up in one discordant mass
and .given the franchise.
And so on through three pregnant para-
graphs.
CHILDREN OF CIRCUMSTANCE, by
lota (cloth, 6s. 6d.). Mrs. Caffyn has fallen
into the familiar “ second book” gulf, and
has written in haste in order that readers
may repent at leisure. There is an un-
satisfactory thread of plot, with little of
“ A Yellow Aster’s ” interest ; and the
rest is pains and padding.
THE GREEN CARNATION (paper, 45.;
cloth, 6s. 6d.) is a particularly bright and
clever skit on Oscar Wilde and the creed
of aesthetic decadence or decadent aesthe-
tics. Many of the smart things are quite
as good as Oscar’s own, and give even the
blas6 reader a laugh. The skit, indeed,
misses its aim—if it has an aim—through
being too good ; for the cleverness of the
wearers of the “ green carnation ” is in
such joyous contrast to the respectable
stupidity of the Philistines that one’s sym-
pathies involuntarily go with the naughty
people who are so nice. The audacity with
which the Philistines’ little tin gods (and
some others) are knocked off their
pedestals is also eminently cheering. For
an appreciative soul the book has decidedly
4s. worth of chuckles.
CAMPAIGNS OF CURIOSITY, by Eliza-
beth Banks (boards, 2s. 5d.), is a reprint
of newspaper articles describing the ex-
periences of an American pen woman who
masqueraded as housemaid, laundry-girl,
crossing-sweeper, and so on. Perhaps the
most interesting papers are those which
shocked Park Lane by telling how many
aristocratic dowagers were ready to launch
the new American rich on the sea of
“ society ” —for a consideration. The
book can be read, but loses most of its
attraction out of London.
“ THE ELECTRICIAN ” PRIMERS (vol.
1, theory; vol. 2, practice ; paper, 2s. 3d.
each) are two handy and valuable books
on the subject of electricity—which, like
other scientific subjects, has a great many
“primers ” that are only intelligible to the
expert. These are simple, practical, and
up-to-date.
STUDIES IN PESSIMISM, by Arthur
Schopenhauer (paper, Is. 3d.) This is a
nice little pocket Schopenhauer, which
gives a pleasant taste of the delightfully
cynical philosopher in half-a-dozen selected
fragments : “ The Misery of Life,”
“ Women,” “ Metaphysics of Love,”
“ Genius,” J&thetics of Poetry,” and
“Education.” Every W.C.T.U. library
should own a copy.
International. Science Series.
This series is classic. The subjects are
treated with solidity and thoroughness by
the most eminent writers of the day ; and if
the books lack something on the side of
popularity, there are none more useful to
the student. Compactly cloth bound, 5s 6d
each, unless otherwise stated.

Blackie’s Modern Cyclopedia.
This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive
publications of its class, it contains an
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume compendium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed
the set of eight volumes for 505., packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia.
The Minerva Library.

published. They are all well printed ; many
of them illustrated ; and the cloth binding is
strong and serviceable. Posted for 2s 6d
each.

A HOME FOR LOST DOGS.
O, take the homeless canine in,
And have him amply fed
And warmly housed 1 It is a sin
To see Atm want a bed.
Yet oh the waste lands many men,
In mud, and rain, and fogs,
Lie down to sleep, and wonderjwhen
They'll treated be like dogs.
Dear Bulletin, —One of the privileges
accorded the Yio. Institute for the Blind has just
been withdrawn by the Turner Govt. Every year
at Christmas charitable people from different
parts of the province have been in the habit of
affording one or more inmates of the Institute a
holiday on their farms for six weeks or two
months. For the past 20 years it has been the
custom of Govt, to give free passes from Mel-
bourne to any of the blind thus travelling; but
this year the Institute has been served with
notice that the privilege will not be accorded in
future! This, of course, will mean that there will
now be no invitations, as the people affording the
holiday can hardly be expected to pay the train-
fare of their guests from Melbourne and back.
Surely to heaven Victoria has not fallen so low as
to be compelled to resort to .so leprously-mean an
economy ! — Shadow.

Aphrodite .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
On a golden dawn, in the morn sublime
Of years ere the stars had ceased to sing,
Beautiful out of the sea-deeps cold
Aphrodite arose—the Flower of Time —
That, dear till the day of her blossoming,
The old, old Sea had borne in his heart.
Around her worshipping waves did part
Tremulous—glowing in rose and gold.
And the birds broke forth into song most sweet,
And flowers born scentless breathed perfume :
Softly she smiled upon Man forlorn,
And the music of love in his heart did beat,
And down to the pit went his gods of gloom,
And Earth grew bright and fair as a bride,
And folk in star-worlds wondering cried—
“ Lo, in the skies a new star is born !”
O, Beloved, thus on my small world you
Rose flashing it all with rosy flame !
Changing sad thoughts to a singing throng,
And creating the earth and the sky anew !
As Love you appeared—and, 10, you are Fame !
And, all my follies and sins despite,
You yet, Beloved, may see my light—
Small, but a star—midst the stars of song.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

Dear Bulletin, —All very fine to laugh
about ghosts, but I believe in them myself, not
that I have ever seen one, but I have felt their
presence for all that. It happened thus : Some
years ago I was in Washington Territory, U.S.A.,
shooting. It was a cold winter and the snow had
driven the elk from the mountains to the valleys
for feed. An old hunter, Hank Richardson,
promised me sport if I accompanied him to a
place called Colseed Bay, near Seabeck. We
started at daybreak one morning, and rowed
over to the Bay, a pull of 12 miles, and
hauled our skiff up on the bank. Then, attended
by a huge dog of Hank’s that was absolutely fear-
less, and would as soon tackle a bear or cougar as
a sheep, We slacked off through the woods on our
quest, intending to return to the skiff before dark,
and reach Seabeck that night. About four in the
afternoon we got our first shot, a fine elk, and, by
the time we had him skinned and the antlers off,
it was too dark to return, and we decided to camp
out for the night. It was bitterly cold and snow-
ing hard, and 1 remarked to my companion
that I wished I was comfortably housed.
“There is a house not a mile from here,”
said Hank, “but it’s haunted, and I wouldn’t
stop there a night for a million dollars.”
And then he told me that a settler, his wife and
family, had been murdered there by Indians 11
years previously, and had haunted the place ever
since; fearful screams were heard at night, and
no one would take up the vacant homestead,
although there were 20 acres of cleared land,
which was open for anyone to claim. I was
younger then and not much given to fear, and
while I could get a roof over my head wasn’t going
to stay out in the snow, and so, finding I could
not budge Hank, started off by myself, going
according to his direction, and I took the dog, at
his earnest request. I found the hut all right—an
ordinary log structure such as the settlers usually
lived in, with a good sound shingle roof, and I
soon had a roaring fire, and was about settling
down for a snooze when the dog startled me with
a fearful howl. I will never forget the appearance
of that dog; with hair erect, tail between his legs,
and eyes starting out of his head, he retreated to
a corner, biting at something I could not see.
Now, I could not see anything but I could feel
someone was in that hut, just as you can feel some-
one creeping up behind you, although you can
hear or see nothing. I was terribly scared and
broke out into a sweat, my knees knocked together,
and my teeth chattered. I could stand it no
longer and rushed for the door, which I had no
sooner opened than, with a blood-curdling howl,
the dog was out before me. Now, tell me this,
even allowing I was a coward, what was the
matter with the dog V Next day neither blows
nor entreaties would get him within a quarter of a
mile of that hut. —Yours, C.E.H.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required —•
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
Th is offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not xoish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Jilach and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for s tine on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, SI Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal \ The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :—Jt. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 22, 1894.
“The Bulletin’s ” Xmas Sermon.
Christmas is a season with a literature that
is peculiarly its own. It is, in the main, a
bathetic variety of literature—a mild and
sentimental drivel in which the flesh and the
spirit meet and maunder together to no
especial purpose. Nineteen hundred Christ-
mases ago, according to a rather vague and
uncertain chronology, there was born into
this world a great Teacher who told his
generation of a hard and stony road which
led to Paradise. The world has since
utterly departed from that Ancient Way
in fact, only an infinitesimal section of man-
kind ever attempted to follow it, and even
that small fraction promptly abandoned the
attempt, and neither it nor its descendants
made any serious effort to return thereto.
Therefore, rather a ridiculous aspect is
presented to the logical observer by the
religion, or rather the innumerable reli-
gions named after that Teacher of old ;
and the Christmas literature of to-day,
which is largely made up of feeble
attempts to reconcile God and Mammon, is
a sad and washy thing of no especial account.
For the fact is that Christianity is practi-
cally dead and has hardly ever been other-
wise, and while modern civilisation lasts it is
not likely to be resurrected. Moreover, no
one seriously expects that it will be resur-
rected, for it and existing civilisation are
antagonistic elements, and it is necessary to
lot one of them go if the other is to be
retained. In respect to this matter, the
unanimity and promptitude with which the
great mass of mankind has decided which
to let go are little short of marvellous,
• • •
The leading tenets of Christianity, as
set forth in the New Testament, are
the doctrines of contentment and poverty
and non-res'stance, and utter contempt for
the things of this life. It is an austere
and uncompromising faith to which every
element of nineteenth - century exist-
ence is absolutely and irreconcilably op-
posed. A nation whose political course
was guided by the Sermon on the Mount
would become extinct within a year. And
as it is with nations, so it is with individuals.
The person to whom his creed is a reality
can be told—or rather he cannot be told, for
such a person is hardly ever found—prin-
cipally by negative evidences. He is never,
by any chance, a successful trader, for the
successful trader, in these days of hard com-
petition, must live by sweating his underpaid
employes, and driving hard bargains with all
who come in his way. He cannot be a
journalist, for his creed demands absolute
truthfulness and a fearless denunciation of
all evil, and no pressman who con-
ducted his business on such lines would
remain out of gaol for a week. The law is
not open to him, for the legal profession
consists mainly in upholding injustice for
a fee. And most other roads which lead
to success are shut against him for like
reasons. The military profession consists
mostly of shedding blood in an unjust cause.
Finance in all its branches is the art of grind-
ing the faces of the poor ; the financier who
won’t grind the faces of the poor never gets
his money and is a ruined usurer. Land-
lordism consists in stealing that earth which
is the Lord’s, and blackmailing His creatures
for the right to live on its surface. Almost
every trade, through the pressure of intense
competition, has come to depend, directly or
indirectly, on adulteration, sweating, and
fraud in one or other of its many shapes.
Even the spreading of the alleged Gospel
would furnish no means of livelihood if con-
ducted on a gospel basis ; the original creed
of the old Syrian preacher would scare away
all save those who had no worldly goods to
put in the collection-plate, and the taber-
nacle would presently be seized for arrears
of rent. If there is one trade or occupation,
save the very poorest, the worst-paid, and
the most menial, which can be successfully
conducted in a manner which will square
with the New Testament creed, The Bul-
letin, at all events, has never heard of it
and never expects to.
* » •
Modern civilisation would crumble away
if it seriously endeavored to live up to the
New Testament ideal; and if it had attempted
to live up to that ideal in the past it would
never have had an existence. Whether the
world would have been better or worse for
the want of it, or what manner of thing
would have existed in its stead, are problems
that are not relevant to the present subject.
It is even doubtful if Christianity itself
would have spread to any great extent if it
had preserved its ancient purity. It was
disseminated by blood and violence and con-
quest rather than by teaching. It first began
to get a permanent hold on the great mass of
the people when it assumed such a shape that
a brutal autocrat could follow its tenets and
still hang on to his treasures and pomp and
authority and remain the same brutal auto-
crat as he was before. It consolidated
its power when it began to furnish
billets for bloated prelates, and to grind
the faces of the poor in order to build
gorgeous cathedrals with the proceeds.
If the Church had cast out the bloodstained
rnonarchs who were among its chief
buttresses, and had refused to amass riches
by unholy means or to acquire power by
oppression, and had adhered to its own
doctrine of poverty and liberty and human
equality, the world would probably have
preferred the old paganism merely for the
sake of its eternal gorgeousness. If it had
remained true to its ancient doctrine of
peace, then the pure-blooded Bengali—
almost the only man on earth who has never
been known to fight either in a good cause
or a bad one —would have been its only
eligible convert. The alleged Christian
faith conquered chiefly by stooping to the
level of the pagan, and it has remained more
or less at his level ever since.
* * *
The old-time converts did not take their
religion seriously. Neither do the converts
of to-day. The world vaguely comprehends
that the church which can square with
modern civilisation is a sham and a hollow
pretence, and, while it has no very definite
yearning for the faith as it professes to be,
instinctively recognises that the faith as it
is is an anomaly. Therefore, it regards the
preacher with thinly - veiled contempt and
good humored toleration, and views him as
an old-womanish being of no especial ac-
count. It expects nothing of him or of his
creed. It ejects him from the schools as a
person who is unfit -to teach the young in a
serious spirit; and keeps him out of Parlia-
ment as an individual who is mentally in-
capable of running the State ; and prevents
him sitting on juries as a citizen who is
deficient in the logical faculty. It regards it
as a matter of course that the Church in
general should be the bitter opponent of
every reform ; and when it hears of the
conversion of the heathen and learns that
the converted heathen are butchering each
other after the good old fashion of their
pagan forefathers it hardly even expresses
surprise. No one even professes to ex-
pect that the so-called gospel of peace
will bring so much as a shadow of peace any-
where. The Church is everywhere either
openly or secretly opposed to education ; it
opposes education on the ground that its
own influence will thereby be undermined,
and thus acknowledges that it lives on
popular ignorance ; and wherever education
spreads the Church grows feeble, thereby
proving that orthodoxy and illiteracy are, to
a great extent, convertible terms. Yet the
civilised world continues, in an indefinite
sense, to pride itself on its alleged
Christianity, and at the same time it treats
its own faith, and the exponents thereof,
with open contempt. The only explanation
is that it doesn’t take its own religion
seriously, and isn’t sufficiently logical or
sufficiently introspective to know the reason
why.
* * *
There have been periods, however, in the
dark and evil past, when men did take
their religion seriously. They were mostly
times of desolating war waged with a view
to throwing off the clerical yoke, or
else times of miserable submission, when
the Church was loathed in secret,
but the courage for active opposition
was lacking. But in those lands where
priestly domination has ceased to be a
grievous burden and religious persecution is
almost extinct, the Church has taken on a
new aspect. Its most famous and successful
representatives are buffoons of the Talimage,
Beecher, Spurgeon, Moody, and General
Booth description. The prosperous reli-
gionist of the present day bangs a discordant
drum in the temple, and hangs a gaudy
banner on its holy shrines, and garnishes the
solemn mysteries of the ancient creed with
jokes borrowed from the domain of nigger-
minstrelsy. And while this individual is the
most prosperous representative of the modern
church, by a curious anomaly its most re-
spected apostle, and the one who approaches
nearest to the original type, is probably still
the Catholic priest of the better class, who
lives for his illogical faith, and dies leaving
two coppers in the pocket of an old waist-
coat as his sole estate. But the priest is a celi-
bate who stands outside of humanity, and his
survival is only another evidence that modern
civilisation and ancient Christianity are
incompatible. He is as incongruous in this
nineteenth century as a mailed crusader
would be at Chicago, and he is a success
mainly because he is content to remain
incongruous ; if he assimilated himself to
modern conditions the result would be dis-
astrous. The old, dream like, unworldly
faith of Galilee can’t be made to fit in with
the latter-day life of Europe and America and
Australia ; either the old faith has to go, or
the new life must be wholly reconstructed.
So far, it is the old faith that has gone, and
Christianity is practically extinct.
The Toast of “The Queen,”
Editor Bulletin. —When Anthony Ogden,
Labor representative of Townsville in Queens-
land Parliament, refuses to drink Queen Vic-
toria’s health, one naturally expects “ loyal ”
journals to fume, and it does a fellow good to
hear shocked sycophancy howling. But it
comes with a kind of jolt to find a Bulletin
correspondent whining because Ogden declines
to lick the hand of royalty. Does “ W.W. ”
think republican principles are only to be aired
by stealth, under cover of darkness, or when
tender Imperialistic sympathies cannot possibly
be wounded ? Is a democrat to apologise for
democracy, and hide, like the bats, from the
daylight? No, by Bunyip ! Freedom is a wife
for a man to honor in the eye of the sun, not
a mistress to keep out of sight down a back
alley. I thank Ogden for his public flout of
Monarchy, her menace and insulter.
• * *
“ W. W. ’s ” chief grievance seems to be Ogden’s
alleged “ bad taste.” “ Bad taste ” is the hand-
maid of conventionality, and conventionality
is the fetish of little minds. All the more
reason to admire the big ones that will not bow
to Baal. The superfine superfluities of
“superior” people, though canonised by
custom, are chiefly born of neurosis and
anaemia. Stalwart men, who sweat with body
or brain, pish such cobwebs into irreverent
dustbins. Let us rather have Hotspurs than
popinjays in Australia. I say nothing against
“ good taste ” —the oil of intercourse ; for that
has always a logical basis. But I protest
against the idea that feeble fashion can affix
opprobrium to any act without being called on
to stand and deliver reasons. If the reasons
are reasonable, pass Fashion, and all’s well; if
not, let’s strip the jade of her mask, and turn
her over to the “ W.W.’s.”
# # *
Ogden’s offence I glory in, and I deny that it
included a scintilla of “ bad taste.” True, it is
still customary for public men in Australia —
—even for those who are Republicans at heart
—to give formal lip-service to the glasses which
hold the toast of “ The Queen.” Most of those
who think with Ogden have not Ogden’s pluck
—it needs some moral courage to be the one
erect man in a crawling assembly. Others
drink as a matter of form, and quiet their con-
sciences with the thought that the ceremony is
perfunctory, meaningless. But I believe that
Ogden, when the glass is filled which the
“ loyal ” shibboleth dishonours, hears the hiss-
ing of the champagne-bubbles as the hissing of
venomous serpents. When he looks into that
glass he sees, as I do, the grisly spectre
of Monarchy—a spectre of blood and tears.
The past becomes present; he sees file before
him, in interminable procession, the victims of
the crimes of kings and queens—the outraged
women, the murdered men, the starved and
tortured children —sacrifices on the altar of
Monarchy, whose stones are human lives. He
sees the tree of which Queen Victoria is flower
—the villainous Normans, the cruel Plan-
tagenets, the brutal Tudors, the currish Stuarts,
the hoggish Germans—rooted in slaughter,
nourished on lust, thriving by greed; and he
shudders and sets glass. A man does
not drink blood.
• • •
“W.W.” finds in “good liquor” an excuse
for obeisance to a democratic lie. Yet he would
not for “good liquor” spit on the grave oi
his father, or suffer a taunt to be flung at the
honor of his mother. Are the graves of Free-
dom’s martyrs to be held less sacred? Is hey
honor to be shamed in silence? Should not
e'Very man who halls himself free be jealous of
the -least blot uponv Freedom’s scutcheon ?
Ogden’s small refusal covers a great principle.
There is a story, perhaps, amocryphal, of an
English sailor taken in the Chinese war, and
ordered to kow-toW before a bedizened man-
darin. He answered, with many oaths, that he
Would kneel to no man, and was forthwith
chopped in pieces. I discern in Ogden’s refusal
to drink Queen V ictori A’s health something of
the same heroic obstinacy, ahdT say it is a good
thing for Australia that such a spirit should be
publicly avowed and encouraged? Climatic or
racial tendencies seem to be weakening here, as
they have weakened in America, the dogged
fibre conspicuous in our ancestors—the grit of
He Montfort, Latimer, Hampden. There is
too much of the ignoble weakness which waives
a principle or submits to an injustice rather
than make a fuss. A lot of us, if not cowards,
too often prefer dishonorable compromise to
honorable danger. D’Artagnan and Porthos
disdained to shout “Down with Mazarin !”
but Dumas makes Mazarin admit that he would
have shouted it himself to save his skin. When
Australia has fighting to do, let us hope
she will not have many citizens of the Mazarin
stamp. I pray, on the contrary, that the
Ogdens may predominate; and I trust the
bracing example of Anthony of that ilk will be
more and more imitated by our public men—
who are especially bound to lead the revolt
against the foolish custom of venerating the
symbols of the curse of Monarchy. For though
Anthony Ogden was disloyal to Queen Vic-
toria, he was loyal to Australia and to
humanity.—Yours, Australian.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Poor Farmer.
The Fat Man party in the Victorian Assembly
wailed once too often about the “ poor farmer”
and his prospective sufferings under the land-
tax, and a truly dreadful thing ensued. For
the party didn’t know that its wail was loaded,
and when it finally pulled the trigger and the
thing went off, the recoil left it sitting down in
a stunned condition and too surprised for
speech. Under Premier Turner’s original
proposal, the agriculturist who possessed land
worth—apart from all improvements and over
and above all mortgages —only <£loo was to go
absolutely free; the man with land to the clear
unimproved value of <£2oo was to pay 6s. Bd.
per annum ; and so on. Therefore, the genuine
“ poor farmer ” would pay little or nothing ;
very often nothing. But, one by one, each big
landholder, who was going to be struck for
,£SOO or so per annum, rose to state that he
didn’t care at all on his own account, but that
he felt it his duty to oppose the measure
because it would ruin the poor farmer, till at
last the Assembly began to believe the hollow
fiction, and became so much moved by the
alleged woes of the alleged agriculturist that it
granted an exemption up to .£SOO. This was a
truly awful blow. The poor farmer is to pay
absolutely nothing, therefore the big landholders
of the Assembly, who objected solely on his
account, have the bottom entirely knocked out
of their objection. The intention was, of
course, to induce the small agricultural crowd
to join the huge monopolists in rejecting the
Bill by pointing out its so-called “ruinous”
character and appealing to their instinct of self-
preservation ; but the monopolists declared so
often that they didn’t object at all on their own
behalf that at last they were taken at their
word. Any party that was one shade less
wooden-headed must have foreseen this inevit-
able result. A decently-intelligent Fat Man
party would have said nothing at all about the
poor farmer, and have done its best to push the
Bill through with no exemptions whatever, and
then it is just possible that the bucolic person
might have felt so bad about his own small
share of the tax that he could be induced to
vote for its repeal at an early date. Now, how-
ever, he has shoved the whole burden on to the
Fat Man at the Fat Man’s own instigation, and
if the Bill only gets through the Council the
poor farmer will find that it is such a pleasant
and convenient way of raising revenue, that he
will presently begin to think of raising more
revenue in the same fashion. The Fat Man
has, quite unconsciously, been sooling his own
dog on himself all this time, and now his feel-
ing are too deep for expression.
Wanted —Australia for the Japs.
Australia’s bogey has changed its mask, and
from Chinese become Japanese. The incidents
of the current war establish definitely and, to
all appearance, finally a conclusion which a few
well-informed people had previously reached,
but which was opposed by almost the whole
vague mass of popular opinion—the conclusion
that the Chinese are not a fighting race, that
they are not united by any spirit of patriotism,
and” that individual apathy and national cor-
ruption make them incapable of any organised
effort for the common benefit. The Chinese
scare is over; but it looks as if soon, with much
more reason, a Japanese scare will succeed it.
For East, North, and West, the Japs are pour-
ing into Australia. At Port Darwin and
Thursday Island they have established a virtual
monopoly of the pearl-fisheries ; at Mackay,
Geraldton, and Townsville many hundreds
have been introduced as labourers on the
sugar-plantation ; and in Westralia, w hile the
men come to fish for pearls, the Japanese
women come by scores to fish for men. The
Jap is patient, intelligent, and adaptable ; and
it is only a matter of time till he gets a per-
manent footing of which it will not be easy to
dispossess him. For the chances are that
Japan—enthusiastic, warlike, and patriotic —
will not submit to be treated by Australia as
contemptuously as China has been. It is quite
conceivable that the rights of a Japanese sub-
ject in Queensland will soon be matter for
international diplomacy, and that we may be
asked to redress grievances at the muzzle of a


rifle, Everything goes to show that Australia
has only two alternatives —free welcome or
complete exclusion. The former is unthink-
able by any democrat. With all his virtues,
the Jap is a cheap laborer of the most un-
Eleasant kind; his race-quality does not fit
im for intermarriage on equal terms with
Caucasian peoples; and his presence in large
numbers will unquestionably degrade the stan-
dard of Australian civilisation. The fact that
in many vital points of character he is equal, or
almost equal, to ourselves, makes his rivalry all
the more dangerous. Yet Great Britain, true
to the worship of the golden calf, has just con-
cluded with Japan a treaty which throws open
Japanese markets to her trajle, and in return
concedes to Japanese subjects the right of entry
and settlement on British soil. It is provided
that any of the Australian provinces may be
made parties to the treaty if they express a
desire to that effect at any time within the next
two years ; and already the journalistic organs
of commercial opportunism are pointing out the
great benefit which would result to Australia
from extensive meat and wool export to
Japan. It is a benefit which should be refused
without the slightest hesitation. Trade is too
dearly bought by the ruin of national life. Men
are worth more than money. Australia will
never sell her birthright for a mess of pottage.
The Central Division.
The difficulty between the two Houses of the
N.S.W. Legislature concerning the throwing
open to selection of the Central Division, raises
once more the old question—what is the real
necessity for throwing open the Central Divi-
sion at all ? According to the Bill as passed by
the Assembly, the Government is to be author-
ised to resume one-half of any, or every, pas-
toral lease, the squatter being compensated for
the loss. The compensation, in its various alter-
native shapes, would probably amount in the
end to a very serious responsibility, but this is
not the question immediately at issue. The
Council took hold of the measure and
amended it to read that land-resumptions are
to be made only at the option of the pastoralist,
under which system the pastoralist will natur-
ally offer to surrender to the State only the
useless, barren and inaccessible parts of his run,
and need not surrender any part at all,
and the opening up of the Central Division, so
far as the purposes of agriculture are concerned,
will be as remote as ever. The case on one
side is that it is urgently necessary to find more
room for agricultural expansion ; on the other,
it is urged that the proposed change, if it
answers the purposes for which it is intended,
is likely to seriously diminish the output of
wool, and the wool export is still the principal
support of New South Wales. There is much
solid logic in this latter contention. In The
Bulletin’s opinion, it is not the Central but
the Eastern Division of the province which
most wants to be thrown open. There is still
enough good land lying unused within a rea-
sonable distance of the sea-board to supply all
the demands of the agricultural community for
at least a few years to come. It is held by
large landholders and syndicates, either for the
purpose of exacting exorbitant rents, or else
to sell at a rise when its value has been
enhanced by other men’s labors and by the
increase of settlement. Its owners produce
nothing, and add nothing to the public wealth,
while the squatter, despite his offensive arro-
gance and all his other bad qualities, is still, in
a sense, a producer and a person of some value
to the community. Moreover, at the present
time the large markets for agricultural produce
are all on or near the eastern coast ; prices are
woefully low ; and railway freights, even if
reduced to the minimum, render it almost
impossible for the agriculturist who is situ-
ated far inland to make anything like a
reasonable subsistence. Under these conditions,
the opening up, on a large scale, of the Cen-
tral Division to a race of small farmers is
very like offering a stone instead of bread.
If the Reid Government will draft a measure
which will have the effect of breaking up the
big estates in the Eastern Division and forcing
them into the market on moderate terms, it
could then afford to leave the hopeless and
desolate Central districts alone until the
beginning of next century. The difficulty with
the Upper House would probably be just about
as great in one case as in the other, but the
results to the community at large would be
widely different. This argument, by the way,
is addressed, not to the Council but to the
people. At present the people have decided
that they want the Central district thrown
open, and it should be th own oieu ac< rd-
ingly.
Proputty’s Effrontery.
The mischievous imitation of the English con-
stitution with which New South Wales started
self-government continues to make itself dis-
liked. The Bulletin devoutly hopes that it
will be more and more disliked until a people
weary of the shibboleths of its great-great-
grandfathers rises in disgust to sweep them
away altogether. The trouble now, as usual, is
with that part of the imitation called the
Legislative Council. In any reasonable scheme
of representative government, the Legislative
Council, as now constituted, could not possibly
find a place, for it represents nothing whatever
but the whim, or the friendship, or the policy,
or the financial necessity, of a Minister
who by chance got the power to make
his brother Bill one of the permanent
rulers of New South Wales. Were it not for
custom, the spectacle of three dozen doddering
old gentlemen—some of whom ought to be in
an asylum, some in gaol, and some in bed—
sitting like a wart on the nose of representative
institutions, would at once be closed by a con-
scientious management. Even when it does
nothing the Legislative Council is ridiculous.
When it does something it is atrocious.
And it now presumes to arrogate equal
powers with the Legislative Assembly,
elected by the people on a manhood suf-
frage basis ; and to flout the decision of
the majority of a just-elected Parliament.
The Assembly’s resolution empowering the
Government to resume half the squatter’s leases
in the central division of the province, when
made necessary by the progress of settlement
and on payment of compensation, was generally
regarded as a compromise highly favorable to
the squatters. There is no need of argument
to prove that. But the Legislative Council,
composed largely of squatters, decides that
where “ our runs ” are concerned— i.e. , the land
leased from Government at a nominal rent and
monopolised as sheepwalks—the popular will
counts for nothing. Thus when, after many
weary years of effort, New South Wales at last
succeeds in amending her land laws so as to
give the small settler a new chance to justify
the hopes which are entertained of him ; when
an Assembly of people’s representatives, fresh
from an appeal to the country, emphatically
voices the popular will; a Council of irfbsponsible
nonentities cheerfully waves people and Parlia-
ment aside, and decides that its private
interests are to be the law of the land. The
occasion is an excellent one to settle for all
time the hash of the Legislative Council.
Were a score of sturdy boilermakers and car-
penters made Councillors in Maoriland fashion,
pending constitutional amendment, the idea
that the Government and people of New South
Wales were created to make “ our runs ” profit-
able would disappear finally. The Bulletin
regrets that Premier Reid is not the man to
do it.
The Logic of Landlordism.
Discussing the rights of the landlord class in
Yic. Parliament the other night, Trenwith
pertinently asked ; “ Supposing Victoria was
owned by one land monopolist, and he ordered
all the rest of the population off his property,
would they go?” “They would,” ejaculated
capitalist Harper—-Victoria’s political blind
Harper—“ if he had a good army to defend his
right.” Trenwith, however, missed the real
point at issue, which is : “ Supposing Victoria
was owned entirely by one big monopolist, and
he ordered all the rest of the people off his pro-
perty, should they go ?” The law, and the Fat
Man’s political economy, and the Tory creed in
general, and the whole doctrine of private land-
lordism say in a loud voice that they should.
All the axioms of that political faith whose
creed is summed up in the words, “ Keep off
the Grass,” declare that they should go at once
and without question. And Harper, vrho
speaks of the landlord defending his “ right,”
also says so—he claims that the wholesale and
retail ejection of everybody is not an unjust
privilege, but a right concerning which there is
no dispute. If the landlord in question owned,
not Victoria merely, but the wdiole solid earth,
and he gave the entire human race legal notice
to get off his property, it would be the duty of
the human race, according to Harper’s logic, to
wade into the sea at once and come back no
more. And if the landlord owned the sea also,
then it would be their duty, under the like
circumstances, to disembark into the upper
atmosphere and go into space. The complete
and wonderful beauty of landlordism can only
be fully appreciated when it is thus worked out
to its logical conclusion. The aggregate land-
lord, it is true, in consideration of a certain
amount of blackmail, has so far consented to
refrain from exercising his legal right to eject
the rest of the human race out of the globe, but
his purchased forbearance makes no difference
in the moral aspect of the case. The circum-
stance remains that he has the legal right. And
the fact that the aggregate landowner should
be authorised to order the rest of the human
race to clear off the earth at his pleasure is
quite as unjust and anomalous as was, in times
past, the fact that he had the right to hang
them at his pleasure while they were on it.
The New South Wales Postmaster-General will
probably end by landing his Government in a
few very expensive lawsuits, when some out-
raged business-man, whose legitimate occupa-
tions have been injured by the non-delivery of
letters, arrested under the gratuitous suspicion
that they contained fees for racing and gamb-
ling-sweeps, resorts to legal proceedings.
Though the Postmaster-General claims to con-
tract himself out of all liability for failure to
deliver letters posted according to the regula-
tions, there are limits even to his authority.
It is held by some that the Postmaster-General
does not come within the legal definition of a
“ common carrier,” and, whatever may be the
legal valueof that contention, if judgment is to be
made upon Joseph Cook individually, it would
seem that he, at any rate, is a most uncommon
carrier. Business firms dealing with suspected
turf-gamblers have had their invoices consigned
to the Dead-letter Office ; an Agricultural
Society complains respecting the diversion of
its business correspondence ; and the long-
haired and meek-faced charity-monger has had
his begging epistles seized because they were
addressed to certain prohibited individuals.
Some day or other some indignant citizen will
proceed to obtain a legal decision as to the right
of Cook, M.L.A., to make foolish mistakes
about his correspondence, and arrest it on the
assumption that it is something which it is not,
and then serious trouble will ensue.
Duncan Gillies stands an even-money chance
of losing his billet as Vic. Agent-General. He
was sent to London by the very man (Patter-
son) who had previously declared that nobody
new was needed at the A.G.’s office as long as
Andrew Clarke was willing to fill the chair
for a modest salary of £2OO over and above his
pension. On Patterson’s own showing the
appointment of Gillies at £2500 per annum
was a sinful job, from the purely financial point
of view. Also, it is generally admitted that
Gillies ought to be recalled immediately, for
the excellent reason that he is Gillies. His
personal claims to be abolished by Act of Par-
liament are beyond question. The Agent-
General billet may continue to exist in an
amended form, but the idea of a busted com-
munity paying £2300 a year extra to keep up the
( Allies fiction is altogether revolting. Gillies
must be fetched back to perish in obscurity.
The decision of the S.A. Labor-party to sup-
port only candidates who are eligible for
admission to a trades union shuts out Premier
Kingston, who is the mainstay of the Demo-
cratic cause in Parliament. At next election
the Labor-party has, by this decision, pledged
itself to oppose Kingston because he isn’t a
tailor or a cooper, and to do its best to throw
him out. If he is thrown out there is no man
at present in sight who can fill his place. If he
gets in, despite all opposition, he will probably
come back with his Democratic aspirations con-
siderably cooled, if not entirely extinguished,
by his party’s ingratitude. Yet the same crowd
which thus deliberately abjures Kingston follows
him in the Assembly on all important questions ;
and if it fails to throw him out at next election,
it will come back and follow him some more.
And if, when it comes back to follow him, after
its unsuccessful effort to eject him altogether,
he refuses to lead, it will probably call him
“renegade” and other words to the same
effect. The S.A. Labor men are doubtless
going to work the right way if their ambition is
to form a Labor-party which will represent
tailors and shoemakers only ; if they want to
create a national Democratic party which will
represent the country at large their proceedings
are suicidal.
What right had Griffith, 0.J., when John
Willard, ex-M.P. for the Tweed (N.S.W.),
was acquitted of the charge of larceny brought
against him by Q. Postmaster-General, to tell
a person w'hom the jury declared innocent that
“ he might consider himself very fortunate
indeed ? ” When a man has been tried and
acquitted, the Judge wdio abuses his power to
blacken the accused’s character degrades the
ideal of justice and proves his unfitness to be
Judge. The situation is not improved by the
fact that Willard, as a political partisan, is
bitterly hated by the ex-Premier’s faction.
Supposing Griffith had been impeached for a
breach of public trust for his share in the
notorious Robb case, when, as Premier and
Attorney-General, he consented to his fees as
advocate being fixed at the highest figures
ever known in Queensland, and drew £2778
from the Treasury while receiving .£I3OO
a year as Ministerial salary, would he not have
protested if, when found not guilty by a jury,
the Judge had told him he might consider him-
self fortunate to get off? Mr. Willard’s
alleged offence was the taking of some receipts
for registered letters from the postal depart-
ment. The jury found that he did not take
them ; the Judge went out of his way to prac-
tically say that he did. If a Judge is allowed
to so misconduct himself, what is the use of a
j ur y ?
Shearing is over in Australia for another year,
and with it its annual concomitant—the strike.
Speaking generally, the Pastoralists’ Unions
have been victorious in all the provinces except-
ing New South Wales, and there, despite all
denials, they have had the worst of the contest.
In Victoria 13s. per 100 was the ruling rate; in
Queensland, 15s. ; but in New South Wales the
standard rate of 20s. was pretty well main-
tained, though a few sheds paid only 17s.
However, the pastoralists, though nominally
the victors, have come off badly on the whole.
They are beginning to realise that the few
miserable pounds that they save on the shearing
rate do not compensate for spoiled fleeces and
ruined sheep, and nights of sleepless anxiety
concerning fires; not to speak of the cost of
landing the gentle blackleg safely on the
ground, and of guarding him when he is there.
In a word, the P.U. is slowly coming to the
conclusion that the amateur blackleg doesn’t
pay. Unluckily, the pastoralists, like most
other people, have short memories. The con-
clusion they have just arrived at is the same
one which they reached and forgot again on
previous occasions, so next year the same old
conflict, with the same old repentance to follow,
will probably begin all over again.
♦
Two returned Rue Australians called in at The
Bulletin office last week to curse unre-
servedly, yet more in sorrow than in anger,
Don W. Lane and all his works. They were
a sturdy pair of Queenslanders, without a cent,
in their combined pockets, and they told
pathetically how they had sold their horses and
their houses and their allotments of land,
and placed their all in Don Lane’s hands, and
how that remarkable dreamer had apparently
gone and blewed it beyond the hope of
redemption. Then they went on to explain
how, when they saw Lane riding about with
revolvers in his belt to superintend the evic-
tion of the natives from their poor little
houses and orange groves, their blood and bile
rose together, and they swore that they would
have no share in such proceedings. So
they “ conveyed ” a settlement boat under the
nose of Alf. Walker, and with two mates
started on a river voyage of 1300 miles, over
snags and rapids, through mud and mangroves,
where the cordial and sociable alligators
clustered so thickly that they had to take
refuge at night in the trees to escape them.
By-and-by they got to the coast and worked
their passage to Australia in different vessels,
meeting in front of Sydney Post-office to hear
that Australia is covered with wrecked
families who have sold up their homes to go to
Paraguay and can’t get a passage from the
bowelless Association, which has 13 balance-
sheets to show instead of cash. And now they
are starting the world again, mighty thankful
to get hack to Australia, and vowing that many
a time they have had reason to remember The
Bulletin’s words that “it'was easier to get to
Paraguay than to get back.” From all which
it appears that Don Lane, in his wild
endeavours to abolish human misery, has only
succeeded so far in making a very substantial
contribution to the size of the original heap.
Melbourne Divorce Court does its business in
a most erratic fashion nowadays. It has bursts
of what looks like altogether unaccountable
suspicion, and when it is in one of these moods
it requires a most surprising amount of evidence
before it will grant a separation even in the
simplest kind of undefended case. Sometimes
half-a-dozen apparently reliable witnesses will
appear to corroborate petitioner’s statement,
and, though there is no testimony whatever on
the other side, the case will be dismissed because
of “ insufficient proof.” And now as to the
genesis of these spasms. There has arisen in
Melbourne a class of solicitors who take divorce-
cases by contract. They undertake, after re-
viewing the evidence, to procure a separation
for a certain fixed sum, and naturally the
lower the expenses can be kept the more profit
there is for the contractor. Therefore, he
cuts the barrister’s fee down to the utmost,
calls only such witnesses as are absolutely
necessary, and does everything on a scale of
squalid economy which is an insult to the lavish
traditions of the law. In other words, he acts
like a man who is dispensing his own money
instead of his client’s. The common impression
is that the Court has “ dropped ” to this un-
professional conduct, and is sitting hard down
upon it. When the court dismisses the case for
want of evidence, the contractor, who is paid
by results only, loses his fee; and when it
makes the wretched man call twice as many
witnesses as he intended to do, it swells the
expenses to such an extent that the transaction
is a dead loss. These violent fits and starts
lend so much uncertainty to the business that
this feeble attempt at providing cheap law in
the divorce branch of the business will probably
soon be abandoned. Cheap law is a thing that
has to be Put Down.
♦
Wealthy Walter Synnot, of Geelong, has
expended a small fortune in an utterly hopeless
attempt to divorce his lawful wife. They were
married 20 years ago. Three years later the
lady, who,. on her husband’s ex-parte show-
ing which, of course, has to be taken
for exactly what it is worth, was
“ sour, disappointed, and cold,” chilled him
into proposing a deed of separation, which bond
of disunion she has icily adhered to ever since.
There is plenty of evidence that Mr. Synnot
did his best to obtain a renewal of the
original marriage contract, and was desirous
of a reconciliation. Mrs. Synnot, however,
rejected all overtures, and went to England.
Many inducements to return were offered her,
but the lady stood fast by her separation deed,
and simply smiled a refrigerating smile from
afar off. Mr, Synnot had no legal leg to stand
upon in applying for a divorce on the ground
of desertion. The virtuous wife is merely
fulfilling her share of the contract, and
that “law of God,” which the Church makes
so much noise about in matters relating to
marriage and divorce, has nothing to do with
the case. Mrs. Synnot’s defence in this action,
apart from the unanswerable parchment, was a
cynical sneer at the Church theory that mar-
riage w as ordained in the best interests of man’s
natural instincts. The lady charged her hus-
band with having committed improprieties
since he lost her society, 17 years ago. The
legal merits’of this extra defence didn’t come
on for serious discussion, inasmuch as the deed
of separation put petitioner right out of court;
but probably Mr. Synnot’s “improprieties”
(admitted) would have settled his chance, any-
w'ay. Between the law of nature, the law of
divorce, and the law of God, which seem to be
ordering him three different ways at once, Mr.
Synnot fares badly. It is the religious aspect
of his case which chiefly interests The Bul-
letin. Granting that Mr. Synnot was for a
long time anxious to resume cohabitation with
the lady to whom God has joined him, is he
now entitled to a special “impropriety”
license? If so, where can he get it? And who
will license the necessary woman?

A firm of Melbourne solicitors has called upon
Premier Turner to reconsider the awful ques-
tion of taxing British mortgagees wdio have so
kindly “ lent large sums of money to persons in
the Australasian colonies on the security of
land.” The firm of solicitors is not prepared
to say that a tax on these foreign philanthro-
pists is illegal, notwithstanding the moral
wdckedness of it. As a matter of duty to the
country, however, the firm ventures to submit
an idea of what might happen if the “rights ”
of the alien usurer were encroached upon. The
firm reminds Turner that it is above all things
necessary to avoid frightening “ British capi-
talists,” lost, when the Victorian Government
wants to raise another loan, they might suspect
it of an intention to tax the interest, “ a
suspicion that would inevitably have the
effect of making them ask, as a premium
against possible risks, a higher rate of interest
than they would otherwise have been willing to
accept.” With ghastly nonsense such as this
Fatman (and his firm of solicitors) fights the
inevitable. Comment on the tone of the letter,
and its reference to our British benefactors’
“ willingness to accept ” interest from Aus-
tralia, is not necessary. The most objection-
able feature of the epistle is its solemn
stupidity. Victoria never undertook not to
have a land-tax which, in the natural order of
legislation, would affect mortgagees. TheTuRNER
Government is entitled to put a tax on land mort-
gagees even as the Rosebery Government may
add a penny to the British income-tax without
troubling to consider whether there is any Aus-
tralian money invested in the British stocks,
&c., that help to produce the income. A ques-
tion of “ good faith ” doesn’t arise in the
matter. As regards loans, the payment of
interest, free from any sort of taxation what-
soever, wmuld be guaranteed by the Victorian
Government if the British capitalists felt at all
nervous, and the bogus “ possible risk ” hereto-
fore mentioned would vanish. Meanwhile, the
capitalist shows no signs of nervousness.
Premier Nelson (Q.) owed the recent
majority of one (!)—that saved his Government
after a first defeat on Jack Hamilton’s motion of
censure —to the absence from the division of Duffy,
M.P., for Bundaberg, and O’Connell, member for
Musgrave. These electorates have been, of recent
years, the pocket-property of MTI wraith. O’Con-
nell is an unsettled sort of person, but Mike Duffy,
the ex-ferry boatman, was commonly regarded as
a solid democrat. The man who has invested his
savings in land until he becomes a magnate, can
generally be relied upon, however, to stay away
from a division which threatens discomfort to his
brother Fat Man. Duffy’s explanation is that he
rushed out of the Chamber to find O’Connell, and
was just returning with his captive to vote against
the Government, when the Sergeant-at Arms
slammed down the bar in front of him.

Bub anybody who has travelled in the same

The immaculate heroine of a recent southern
society divorce-case, who posed as the badly-per-
secuted spotless plaintiff, made rather a bad
beginning of her resurrected single life. She
made the mistake of putting up at a strictly
fashionable, but rather lively pub., and of enter-
taining a couple of johnnies unawares. The chil-
dren were so fond of Mr. Blank, you know, and
there really was no harm if he gave the youngest
a little gold bangle and took the whole party on
an ice-cream hunt round town —and all this airy
gallivanting while the newspapers were still pal-
pitating with details of her domestic infelicity.
However, on the very eve of a theatre-party
arranged by the lady, the johnnies backed out of
the little picnic, with many polite excuses, and
saved her reputation in spite of herself. By way of
showing the hideous ingratitude of a woman
baulked in the act of a scatter-brained little spree,
she now cuts both of her social preservers as dead
as door-nails.
How is it, I wonder, that the numerous and
occasionally reliable books on “Modern Etiquette”
don’t contain up-to-date hints for well-meaning,
but careless divorcees? The great female army
(for it is only women who are seriously affected by
divorce —your unchained Benedict is as good as
new after it’s all over) which now marches
under the banner of the rule nisi certainly
want just as much social piloting as the
hordes who are getting engaged and mar-
ried with full printed directions and diagrams
to enable them to go through the mill in style.
Yet, so far, there isn’t a word of advice to the
divorced. If ever I write a book on Manners and
Tone, I will include a special chapter on the sub-
ject, in which I will advise the twice-laid spinster
to avoid V-shaped bodices, dress circles, loud con-
versation, champagne, young men and big thumb
rings as piously as she would a shower-bath of
freckles, or else the world will forget who was the
plaintiff, and get horribly mixed up over the ins
and outs. No really fashionable book of social
hanky-panky can now be regarded as complete
without special reference to the deportment of
the divorced. It might begin with a genteel
reference to the Empress Josephine, and east
quite a society glamour over the distressing
business.
Talking about divorce reminds me that a recent
Sydney marriage was a demonstration of the old
gag—“ Everything comes to him who waits.”
For the bride had been loved by the bridegroom
in the romantic and misty past, when she was a
blushing thing of 20, and the world seemed to her
peopled with long, tawny-raoustaohed heroes over
6ft. high, with dreamy violet eyes and tan boots,
all on the jump to strew her path with diamonds.
At that unpromising period the bridegroom pro-
posed and got a scornful refusal from the young
lady who was waiting for the duke. Fourteen
years afterwards, however, he once more came
smiling up to the same old scratch, proposed, and
was gratefully accepted ! In that anxious interim
the heroes had dwindled down to ordinary citizens
of five-feet odd, carrying little black luncheon-
bags, and living on air so as to save up enough to
buy a bicycle, and when Romeo re-appeared (a
little fatter than before), just as the situation was
becoming desperate, he had an odds-on chance.
It all reminds me of a picture I saw somewhere
recording the fact that at eighteen a girl asks
“Who is he?” at twenty-eight “What is he?”
but at forty “ Where is he?”
By the way, a harrowing scene occurred in a
Southern church, the other day, just before a wed-
ding. It was a scene which leaves the Inquisi-
tion nowhere for refinement of cruelty. The
verger was hanging about the steps waiting to
play bell-wether to the wedding-party, when the
bridegroom, unsupported and petrified with fear,
turned up and shied about the entrance uncertain
how to proceed. “What d’yer want,” grunted
the “virgin.” “ I—l am the man,” gurgled the
unhappy creature in lavender pants; but that
simple statement was too vague for his tormentor.
“What man? Best man?” said he. “N —no,”
stammered the hero of the hour, “ I’m going to be
married. Has She come yet?” But these in-
definite third personal pronouns were too vague for
the verger. “Has who come yet?” he said testily.
“Oh, the girl I’m going to marry,” wailed the
victim in desperation—but just then the hired
white steeds were observed in a cloud of dust,
and the situation somehow righted itself.
Sydney’s brand-new Mayor is a quiet and un-
aggressive individual, a printer on a large scale,
with a delicate laissez-faire kind of Mayoress, and
a grown-up daughter who will probably do most
of the pump-handle business at mob receptions.
But it is not anticipated that the citizens will be
worried with very many invitations to the Town
Hall while Mayor Lees is on top, especially with
so much talk going the rounds re the curtail-
ment of the civic salary. Talking about Mayors,
how differently different people regard them.
“What do you think of the new Mayor?” said
one lady to another at the cricket-match. “Well,
we’ll see how she’ll do,’’ was the very Welsh-
sounding reply.
Over Mayor Manning’s retirement I drop croco-
dile tears. He was the jauntiest civic potentate I
have ever seen in the robes picked out with
rabbit skins—his language hardly ever deserved
the epithet of Town Hall English, and his legs
would carry a penny novelette to a flourishing
conclusion. ” Though he was not a butterfly poten-
tate, like one of his predecessors who endeavoured
to float to a title on a high tide of villainous fizz,
Mayor Manning’s “handle” got home just the
same. It arrived there, too, spite of the fact that
his term of office was one of the least festive ever
known in civic annals. But, then, so much depends
for that sort of thing on being backed up by a
sassiety-souled Mayoress.
What a crowd of “delicate” women there are
among the wives of public men. I understand
that a very high political personage in Australia
is simply wild to run a political salon, but his
pretty wife is never out of the doctor’s clutches,
and he might just as well be an unhooked bachelor
for all the social help he gets from his mate. But
politicians seem to have bad luck in matrimony.
Very few of them secure Primrose dames, ready
to rush out and embrace a vegetable Chinaman in
order to secure his vote for the old man—far from
it! Indeed, when a politician pushes his fortunes
to the top of the tree, Mrs. P. generally sits at
the root admiring his fine feathers, without the
faintest idea of assisting him to retain his bad
eminence. Just think of Australian public men—
aren’t they nearly all grass-widowers in the eyes
of society ? Where is the Cabinet Minister
possessed of a wife who is a smart talker, a grace-
ful hostess, or a successful social intriguante, such
as we folks down under are told exist in bushels up
above ?
There is no deception about the interest Aus-
tralians take in cricket. There was almost a
Melbourne Cup multitude at the Sydney Cricket
Ground last Saturday, the first heat of the test
matches, to see whether All-England could hit a
leather ball harder than All-Australia. I thought
the scores looked as if the Kangaroo (going the
entire animal) would give the British Lion toko,
though really I don’t understand the game a bit.
What most interested me about the field was the
extreme difficulty one-.experienced in separating
the sheep from the goats—l mean the “ gentle-
men ” from the professionals —of the visiting team;
they all looked so alike in the open ! No wonder
the team put up at different hotels—they would
never sort themselves to the public satisfaction if
they didn’t. And other objects that amused me
were the exiled Englishwomen, fat and in full
bloom as well as young and slender, sporting the
blood-shot colors of the Briton in hatbands and
neckties. But the patriotic display was quite
superfluous, considering that one always spots a
she-Englishwoman by the noble size of her feet,
and not by the flag in her hat. By the way, 1
didn’t see one society woman or girl sporting the
Australian colors. I suppose it was because some
of the “gentlemen” are so rich and so single,
Sassiety, which always has one longing eye on
Piccadilly, cannot run the risk of jeopardising a
life’s chance for the sake of an Australian blue
hatband !
I suppose there was the usual percentage of
decent dressing on view, but the first person I
jolted against in the Members’ was the wife of a
society publican in blue muslin (I mean the wife,
not mine host), salted with yellow roses, like wall-
paper at a penny per yard ; and the spectacle
warped my judgment for the whole afternoon.
There was a damsel there in a choppy blouse and
skirt of pen-and-ink checks, with spasmodic
appearances of white insertion, which would have
made a good bathing-suit—a garment it cruelly
resembled. Another lady wore rose-pink, with
green sprouts upon her shoulders, and she looked
like the girl (even now upon us) who comes
on and tries to convince you that she’s
a pink rose with a green calyx in the
pantomime. Hilda Sponge, in spook-white
silk, was artistically impressive ; backed up by
Fairy Godmother Essie Jenyns Wood (who, I
hear, is writing a novel). A married lady in pink
had her ear tickled all the afternoon by the en-
croaching tawny moustache of her “Plutonic”
friend. The last time I saw that moustache it
was outlined against a glass door in a most
romantic conjunction.
The Sydney Cricket-ground management broke
out on Saturday into “ rules ” for ladies warning
them in darkling legal language not to sit in the
gangway, and not to open their umbrellas even if
they fried as they sat. Considering that the
members’ stand is only an improved sort of cab-
men’s shelter, and that the women spread out in
interpolated cane chairs half-way over the gravel,
the umbrella ukase is rather severe on girls with
noses addicted to freckling. But woman is a
meek and law-abiding biped, and not one gamp
was elevated, although people beyond the roof-
shade looked like hot toast.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

“Jones,” the third and last production of
the comedy season at Sydney Lyceum, was dis-
tinguished by the fact that there was less of it
than any of its predecessors. It was also distin-
guished by the fact that it was a shade worse than
the "Foundling,” and immeasurably inferior to
the "New Boy.” Apart from these two circum-
stances, it wasn’t distinguished in any way what-
soever. On the opening night it lasted just an
hour and three-quarters, and much of that was
interval, and very cheerful and comforting interval
too. Apart from its brevity, the most original
feature about “Jones ” was the absolute want of
all feminine interest. There were only two ladies
in it, and they had little to do and nothing of any
importance to say. A good deal was said about a
third lady, who seemed as if she would have been
interesting if she had appeared, but she didn’t
turn up. The result proved that a comedy can’t
be built out of male characters only, especially
when most of them are dull and stupid and dis-
mally commonplace. “Jones’’turned upon a small,
ugly, reddish individual, who, for certain reasons
connected with a lady, visited a small and stupid
village in disguise, and, when his business was
completed, threw his disguise into the river and
reappeared in his real character. The stupidest
policeman on earth then found the disguises in the
river, and arrested him for the murder of himself,
and, not being able to produce himself, he was
naturally put in gaol, and talked to death by the
Mayor. When he recovered his disguise, and
resumed his first character, and proved that he
was still alive, his disappearance in his second
capacity couldn’t bp explained, and the authorities
— whose name was Elijah Porkington—demanded
that the matter should be cleared up by both of
him appearing together. Something good might
I e made out of this idea, but the author merely
states the circumstances after the solemn fashion
of a sheep catalogue, and under these conditions
there remained only the bare fact that the hero
was sometimes a small, reddish man with whiskers,
and sometimes a small, brownish man without
whiskers. The company maintained something
very like a dead level throughout, except that
Messrs. Hawthorne and Ralph Roberts rose
above the prevailing mediocrity whenever the
solemn mystery of “Jones” made such a thing
possible.
** * *
“ Cinderella,” with a very strong com-
pany, some more living pictures, and a myriad of
legs, commences at the Lyceum on Saturday.
Mr, Rignold revived the fine old comedy
of “Confusion ” at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), to fill
in the last week before the upheaval of the panto-
mime. Her Majesty’s company, on the rare
occasion when it drops into comedy, generally
drops into this particular comedy, and does it so
well that many people are inclined to wonder why
it doesn’t depart more frequently from the gory
paths of melodrama, and try something of a lighter
character. Even the story of the large, hulking,
but noble son who is unjustly suspected of making
away with the old man, and whose vast estates
are “shook” by a wicked relative acting under a
forged will, begins to pall after a time, despite its
many excellencies. “ Confusion ” will be suc-
ceeded on Saturday by “The House that Jack
Built,” a pantomime which sounds a good deal
less hackneyed than the average, Avery big
company has been laid on for the occasion, and all
the glory and miscellaneous effect which the stage
will hold are to be supplied with a lavish hand.
** * *
The ever-welcome Bland Holt has arrived
in Sydney and deposited his luggage at the Royal,
where he opens on Boxing Night, with the “Span
of Life.” The co. includes Walter Baker, Mrs.
Holt, Misses Hilda Spong, Virginia Vivienne,
and several dozen others. Meanwhile, the tearful
tale of the “Streets of London ” is dragging the
slow wail of its bitter anguish to a conclusion, and
the heroine will presently totter over the pave-
ment in search of a crust for the last time.
** * *
Charles Arnold’s co. opens another short
season of “ Charley’s Aunt ” at Sydney Criterion
on Boxing Night, with Mr. Seagar, in lieu of
Frank Thornton, as the lady from Brazil. The
Kinetoscope show, at 148 Pitt-street, has been
enlarged and added to, and is more than ever
worthy of inspection. A flying-machine is either
to fly or fall into the sharky deep from the point
at Chowder Bay on the 26th inst., and various
concerts and minstrel and other shows are to cast
a radiance over the season of the plum-pudding
and the tough fowl with the scaly blue legs.
** * *
The Jubilee Singers, with the melo
dious Orpheus M‘Adoo in charge, will trill
their pensive lay ao Sydney Centenary Hall on
Christmas and six succeeding nights. The Jubilee
Singers have always left a pleasant memory
behind them, especially the bass gentleman—
whose name The Bulletin forgets just now—who
used to be “ A Hundred Fathoms Deep.” If this
gentleman has left a worthy successor, they should
still furnish a happy medium for those persons
who are not sufficiently frivolous to go to theatres
and not quite dreary enough to find solace in
Pastor M‘Neill.

Ernest Hosking (whose portrait appears
on p. 10 of this issue), is the Melbourne com-
positor whose genius for spook manifestation has
been several times referred to in The Bulletin
during the past half-dozen years. When Dr.
McCarthy started to advertise his simple faith
The Bulletin casually mentioned that a Mel-
bourne wizard could perform all the spiritualistic
fakes of the time, and explain how they came to
pass—for a reasonable consideration. Hosking is
that same wizard. The present writer has an old,
but still vivid, recollection of fixing the gentle-
man up in a black bag in the darkened room of a
mutual friend. A seance committee of two, in-
cluding the writer, placed a hand on either of
Hosking’s knees to make sure that he didn’t shift
from the bag, but before the gas was turned up
again, revealing him in his original helpless con-
dition, the spirits had given the committee
sundry severe prods from the rear, in addition
to a liberal display of rough and ready phenomena.
Hoskings, at that time, was a comparative novice
in the black art, but already he had been ap-
proached, with a view to business, by local hum-
bugs, who told him he was an “ unconscious
medium,” and implored him to participate in their
lucrative swindles instead of giving the game
away gratis. Hosking is just a born trickster,
with a peculiar talent for getting at the secret of
Mellonic or other mysteries. He can master the
details of any sort of “ wonder” show at one sit-
ting.

The deposit receipts of the City of Melbourne
Bank are now for sale at 10s. in the £,, while buyers offer
only Bs. This woeful drop plainly means that some
people who either are, or think they are, “in the know”
nave lost all reasonable hope of the City of Melbourne
pulling through under existing conditions. The Mel-
oourne Board of Works, by the way, has a reconstructed
deposit of £330,000—a part of that loan for which Fitz-
gibbon was so “shamefully squeezed” in London—in
this unhappy institution. If the City of Melbourne had a
little available cash on hand, it could wipe off a lot of its
liabilities at a very cheap rate by buying up its deposits at
present prices, but apparently it hasn’t the cash. Or if
the Turner Government cared to come to its assistance
by offering 2J per cent, debentures to its creditors in lieu
of their claims, the offer, in the present condition of
affairs, would probably be accepted with signs of great
Joy. Then the State could take over the deposits at 3 per
cent., and boss the institution until its liabilities were
discharged. Judging by the stock exchange barometer,
nothing short of this is likely to meet the emergency.
The City of Melbourne represents, at present,
the lowest depth in the banking business. Colonial Bank
deposits sell at 12s. 6d.; Commercial of Australia, 15s.
and (for long-dated deposits) 12s. 6d.; English, Scottish
and Australian vary from 12s. to 15s. 3d.; London, 109.;
Victoria, 17s. 3d.; National. 17s. ; North Queensland,
