Among the Melbourne banks, the National is the only one
whose ordinary shares are saleable. Preference shares of
the City of Melbourne are valueless ; those of the National
(£lO paid) fetch t 6; Victoria (£lO paid), £5; Colonial
(£9 15s. paid), £1 10s. ; Commercial of Australia (£lO
paid), £1 2s. Altogether, the restoration of confidence is
in a worse way than it ever was before.
• » * »
About the last possible disaster has struck the
shareholders of the Bank of New Zealand, in the shape of
a call of £3 6s. Bd. per share. Most of the shares are
already fully paid up, in name at all events, but when the
Maoriland Government came to the rescue of the bank
an Act was passed according to which the liability was
made practically unlimited. Under this new arrange-
ment, in order that a call should be made, the
auditors have only to certify that the bank’s assets
are unequal to its liabilities, or that “an emergency
has arisen which makes a call or calls expedient,” and
apparently the emergency has arisen in the present
instance. The £500,000 which is to be raised is to be
devoted to paying off debentures of the B.N.Z. Estates
Cos., and thereby undoing, in part at least, and at great
expense, one of the most awful pieces of finance in
modern times. The debentures of the Estates Co.—which
company is simply the B.N.Z. under another name—
were floated in London at something like £96 net
for each £IOO debenture; they bear interest at the
exorbitant rate of 5£ per cent., and they were to be
redeemed in the year 1910 at £lO3. The bank, however,
is privileged to pay them off before maturity at £lO5,
and it is cheaper to make even this sacrifice than carry
the ghastly burden any longer. Yet there is a wild
humor about that species of finance by which an institu-
tion has to pay a premium of nearly £7o,ooo—for that is
what it amounts to if all the Estates Co.’s debentures are
taken up—for the privilege of paying its own debts.
When the present call is collected the proba-
bilities are that the Bank of New Zealand will at last find
itself in comparatively smooth water. With its enormous
paid-up capital, however, and its expensive 4 per cent,
preference stock, dividends will probably be very small
for many years to come. One thing is certain, that no
other bank in these provinces has stood so many shocks,
and so many reconstructions, and yet survived. The old
B.N.Z. has, to all intents and purposes, been written off
bodily, and the present institution is entirely new. The
effect upon the shareholders is virtually the same as if
the institution had liquidated in 1888, and anew one had
been started on its ruins. Meanwhile shares are, accord-
ing to the cable, being offered in London on an entirely
new basis, the seller giving the scrip gratis and paying
15s. per share to anybody who will take it away. The
investment ought to be a reasonably good one at the
price.
♦ * * *
The National Mutual Assurance Society’s
balance-sheet reads well, so far as the revenue account is
concerned, considering the awful depression of the past
year. The premium income (£208,000) is only £4OOO less
than in 1893, and the falling-off is entirely owing to sur-
renders by stone-broke policy-holders. The new business
shows a marked improvement. The expenses are practi-
cally the same as last year, and £91,754 have been added
to the accumulated funds of the society. Last year the
addition made was £113,700, but the difference is fully
accounted for by the extra £20,000 paid away during 1894
in surrenders. The interest received on investments
(£73,059) is £2OOO more than last year, and very nearly
covers all claims during the twelve months, and in all
respects the figures show substantial progress.
The investment account, however, shows sundry
features which may be capable of satisfactory explana-
tion, but which, on the surface of tilings, tend to excite
comment. An item of £65,655 for building society sh res
which item, by the way, has increased by £9OOO during
the year —may be thoroughly sound, but considering that
very many building institutions are more or less dubious
in these times, it would be well to give the names of the
societies, in order 10 show that they are among the few
concerns which are absolutely above suspicion. A sum of
£IOI,OOO is on fixed deposit in Victoria, and it wool I be
interesting to know in what bank it is fixed, and bow
much fixed it really is. Some Victorian fixed deposits are
a very feeble kind of security nowadays; others are as
good as can be desired. Also, the item “accrued in-
terest” stands at nearly £19,000. If this mean* both
interest accrued and interest outstanding it is entirely
satisfactory. If it means only what it says, then the
outstanding interest is wholly unaccounted for, and
as it may be safely asserted that an institution
of such dimensions as the National Mutual, with an
income from investments of £73,000 per annum, can-
not possibly avoid having a certain amount of arrears, the
most reasonable inference in this case is that they are
made invisible to the naked eye by adding them to the
amount of the mortgages. Under such circumstances it
is impossible to tell what the arrears amount to, and the
security or otherwise of the investments can’i be esti-
mated. Every insurance society’s balance sheet ought to
show the outstanding interest as a separate item, and to
avoid misconception the National Mutual should seriously
consider this matter.
Another noteworthy asset is “ Deferred instal-
ments of annual premiums on policies in force” t'45,000.
If this means, as it appears to do, that where premiums
are paid quarterly or half-yearly in advance, instead of
annually in advance, the society takes credit for the
balance of the year’s premium as an asset, the proceeding
is open to some question. At all events, no such item
appears in the balance-sheet of any other of the big life
offices of Australasia. Altogether, the accounts of the
National Mutual—like those of many other offices—might
be made a good deal more explicit with advantage.
* * ♦ ♦
The shareholders in the old Bank of South
Australia will probably be struck at an early date with a
call of £l2 10s. per share. When the Bank of S.A. was
taken over by the Union it was confidently anticipated
that there would be a surplus of £BOO,OOO, after making a
very large allowance for depreciation, but the alleged
surplus entirely faded away upon investigation, and
£350,000 worth of liabilities remain to be provided for.
The total disappearance amounts to about a mHlion and a
Quarter. Banks are evanescent things.
n * • * *
One result of the bank panic and consequent
commercial paralysis in Newfoundland is that £500,000
worth of unsaleable cod-fish are rotting in the stores and
degenerating into a feaftome mass of corruption. Most
banking panics have an ancient and fish-like odour
attached to them, but such a smell as this is unprece-
dented.
• * ♦ *
The case of William M’Lean and the Federal
Bank has started again in Melbourne, but just as the
examination reached the most interesting point, Judge
Hodges, on the application of M'Lean’s counsel, ordered
the court to be cleared, and the rest of the case is to be
heard in strict privacy. Therefore, even the glimmer of
light which seemed likely to be thrown on the Federal
Bank mystery has been withdrawn, and the public is left
to form its own conjectures as to the for all this
gloom and secresy. Most people are of opinion that the
case is one which should have been heard in the Town
Hall, with free admission for everybody, so that the spot-
less purity of the defunct institution could have been
vindicated as publicly as possible, but somehow this view
doesn’t commend itself to the crowd which is laboring lor
the restoration of confidence.
* * * *
B.H. Proprietary has purchased the British
concentrating plant, and proposes to erect it as part of
the sulphide plant of the new concentrating mill. When
this portion of the mill (capacity, 1400 or 1500 tons sul-
phide weekly) is equipped, the big mine will make its first
plunge in sulphide-treatment on a large scale. The
British concentrating plant, which has been idle for 18
months, is chiefly remarkable as the most eminent
example of the genus white elephant on a field abounding
with such
The smelter fumes difficulty promises to bring
the Broken Hill Proprietary Cos. into trouble before long.
The nuisance has been patiently tolerated by Broken Hill
hitherto, but the increased proportion of sulphide now
smelted causes a dreadful odour, as if the earth was all
one bad egg; and matters are coining to a head. Yet the
fumes are probably not now any more deleterious—only
more pungent —than before. The Lead Commission,
which sat when more smelters were running than at
present, found that about 15 tons of lead were volatilised
daily, a large proportion of which fell within a compara-
tively small radius of the smelters.
« ♦ ♦ ♦
Four years ago Schlapp, then metallurgist, said
that nobody would live in Broken Hill when they began
to smelt the sulphides unless the fumes were condensed.
The statement proves at least partially true. The sad-
dest feature of the lead-fume poisoning is that children in
the town (disastrously pitched right under the hill) are
most seriously attacked. Here is a typical case. A
widow, working as a charwoman, with five children, has
four of them afflicted. Two are bed-ridden, another has
brain-paralysis, the fourth has one side partially para-
lysed. Broken Hill will yield Australia a plentiful supply
of distorted idiots.
* • • •
The deep prospecting work comprised in the
crosscut from the 1330 ft. level of the Extended Company’s
shaft, East towards the Broken Hill lode, being expensive
and practically hopeless, was lately suspended by the con-
tributing companies (Central, Block 10, and Extended).
The Central Cos., whose ground the crosscut was passing
through at the time of the stoppage, has since bored with
a diamond drill 140 ft. from the end of the crosscut,
reaching a point 800 ft. below the bottom of its own main
shaft. This work has since also been abandoned as hope-
less. The total distance prospected by crosscut and bore
was 680 ft.

After spending over £BOO in connection with
the Victorian mining show, and bringing judges from
remote parts of the province to report on the various
machines, the Victorian Mines Department is actually
unable to make any awards at all in connection
with the first-class gold s iving appliances, owing, it
is reported, to a ridiculous bungle with the tailings
from the different machines, said tailings having been
mixed up somehow, so that no one knows where each
sample belongs to. One exhibitor spent over £125 in
connection with the show, and has been waiting patiently
for five weeks for his expected prize. He will probably
have to wait 50 years now. Some of the exhibitors talk
about sueing the Department for damages.

The Garibaldi, the last surviving quartz mine in
the Ruthenrlen district (Victoria) has gone under. Share-
holders refused to respond to calls. Rutherglen was a
great gold producing district for years, but it is now
practically extinct.

Black Flag is the last small Westralian sensa-
tion. On each side of an enormous quartz blow, which
had heeu napped to no purpose a thousand times by dry-
blowers and others, magnificent specimens have been
found. Lord Sudeley has acquired the main show for
£30,000.

Last Mount Drysdale (N.S.W.) crushing aver-
aged lOoz. The directors have decided to put up two
five-foot Huntingdon mills, and to increase the output.

Cabled that the directors of Bayley’s West Cos.

paying gold-mines of Tasmania. It has opened up a well-
defined ore body at 400 ft. ; the first crashing at New
Pinafore battery has proved it good enough to pay divi-
dends and to justify the erection of crushing machinery.

A Mr. West lately came all the way from Lon-
don to Melbourne to get his name removed from the
share register of Chaffey Bros., Limited, in connection
with anew issue of shares which he wished to repudiate.
He won his case and was awarded £l4O for travelling
expenses. For Chaffey Bros, it was argued that West
might have stayed in England and given his eiidance on
commission, but the learned Judge pointed out that the
expense of a commission would have totted up to about
£l4O, anyway.

A leading Melb. softgoods firm appears to be in
trouble. A writ for £27,000 has been issued against it by
a well-known firm of merchants, this sum representing
the amount of 70 promissory-notes.
* » * *
The three sales held by auction of the City of
Melbourne Building Society’s property have realised
£97,000. The terms of purchase were 10 per cent, down,
and balance at SO days. Bidding was brisk and buyers in
the majority of cases paid very fair values for the lot*.
This, and other transactions in Melbourne recently,
knock the stuffing out of the Fat Man’s argument against
the proposed property-tax—that “property has no ascer-
tainable value”: but the Argus goes along as usual,
bumping its head against solid facts, yet totally unim-
pressionable in its fatuousness.

Nil Desperandum (White Feather) reports :
“ Reefs so large that we are able to break out 2owt.
blocks. Dry blowers finding rich heavy gold slugs on and
near the lease. You will see from the foregoing that the
reef is developing in a most extraordinary manner.
The writer quite fails to see It. There is no mention of
the quality of the 2cwt. blocks, which may be simply road
metal, and the gold which is found “ near the lease
doesn’t seem to have any more value to the Nil Desper-
andum Cos. than the gold in a bank has to the other bank
in the next street.

A sample of stone from the New Victoria mine,
Coolgardie, treated at Bendigo, went 18oz. to the ton by
battery, and 240 z. by chlorination. The New Victoria is
one of'the best, shows on the field, but if its shareholders
expect anything like this average in bulk, they will be
sadly disappointed. It looks like a good 3oz. reef, and is
big enough to pay divs. at that.

An alleged mine in the Kurnalpi district, called
the Macintosh, is being hawked about for sale in London.
It is described as being in full working order, with a
battery of 15 stamps and abundance of stores to carry on
a going concern. Now, if this is so, and the mine a
three-ounce one as alleged, why go to England for
capital? Isn’t the mine grinding out its own capital day
and night. When a 15-head battery is pouring onr
wealth at the rate of 3oz. to the ton, it is its own capital.

The writer has heard of a 10-ton Coolgardie
sample which has gone “ ’Ome,” and has also heard of 111
men being engaged many days selecting it from a heap
10 times as big. If this is true, Jhen the sample shoe <t
contain two-thirds of the gold in that 100 ton heap. You
can pick even Coolgardie stone to death.

N.S.W. Judge Foster, who has been more
or less incapacitated by illness ever since he
mounted the bench, now definitely retires from
the Supreme Court. G. B. Simpson, Attorney-
General in the Reid Cabinet, takes his pi ice ;
while Jack Want, who a little while ago announced
that he was sick and tired of politics, becomes
Attorney-General. Foster has been a bad specu-
lation for the taxpayer ; he was too feeble for his
billet, and barely served half the prescribed tir n
before he was compelled to “ accept ” his pensu n
and become an honorable and reasonably pi;
turesque burden on his suffering country.
Simpson is also far past the prime of life, and, in
the natural order of things, his retirement on a
pension, owing to physical weakness, should
already be almost in sight. Judgeships, in N.S.
Wales, are now, to a great extent, a reward for
living to an advanced age and being a respectable
invalid, and, in the interests of economy, it is
about time for a new departure.
Author Robert Louis Stevenson died sud-
denly of brain paralysis at Samoa on Dec. 3, aged
44. An invalid from his youth, he sapped his
scant energy at the toilsome trade of letters, and
has suffered 10 years’ more life than reasonable
expectation would have granted. Yet though he
bravely bartered Being for Seeming, the fame
which rewarded him came too soon to stay, and
his last days were clouded by the suspicion that
he had outlived a reputation which he never
wholly merited. The Stevenson cult was chiefly
founded on the arts of academic reclame to which
the Andrew Lang coterie owes so much. It was
upheld by the praise of Mr. Gladstone, who is
everything but a good critic ; and by the admira-
tion of the literary school which prefers the play of
words to the display of worth. The obedient public
echoed the applause of its leaders ; but Stevenson,
who was only a philosopher in his books, may be
counted fortunate in living to see but the be-
ginning of Time’s revulsion. Little of his work
will linger long after him. He owed nothing to the
originality of genius, everything to the toil of
talent. He strove hard to satisfy the clamour of
his friends for a great book, but its material was
not in him ; and even that public taste which he
himself describes as “a mongrel product, out of
affectation by dogmatism,” would not be put off
with the showy performances in which he tried to
make style do duty for thought. His novels are
mere juvenilities, pleasant and impermanent. His
best work is in his essays, which embalm some of
the most exquisite prose in the English language.
But Stevenson’s art, with all its miraculous imita-
tion of simplicity, never reached the strength of
nature. His work is marvellously gilded, but it is
not gold. It was pathetic to see him struggling in
his Samoan refuge to produce the masterpiece
which his premature notoriety demanded by way
of justification. He taxed himself to the utmost,
and now the poor overdriven brain has given way,
and his memory remains, not indeed that of the
great writer which he hoped to be, but that of a
clever man of letters who has done good service in
his day and generation.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Personal Items.
Thomas Bent, once Speaker of the Vic-
torian Assembly, was 56 years old on Dec. 17.
N.S.W. Premier Reid suffers much from
nightmare. His most frequent nightmare is
Parkes.
The proprietor of the Queanbeyan (N.S.W.)
Observer is now a Mrs. Fablick. Our lovely
contemporary.
The case of Dibbs and his evil genius only
shows once more what successful men some men
might be if some other men had never been born.
A recent Victorian candidate’s £5O deposit
was promptly garnisheed by his tailor. He paid
off the tailor, but it was attached again the same
day by his landlord.
About the most warlike of Maoriland’s
military men is a manufacturer of paper bags,
produced exclusively by girl labor. The next
most warlike man is a draper.
Chief J ustice Higinbothara once said of a
high Australian legal dignitary : “ I certainly was
at school with him, but 1 never could see anything
in him but a bow and a smile.”
Postmaster-General Cook has just com-
pleted his 34th year and the eighth of his residence
in Australia, “Luck has nothing to do with a
man’s advancement; it is pluck and brains that
do it.”
That Polynesian savant, Rev. Lorimer
Fison, has just returned from a visit to England,
his first visit for 37 years. He finds decadence.
“The smoke of great cities, with sheep-runs in
between.”
The most memorable, but least remem-
bered, achievement of the late Rev. C. F. Garnsey,
of Sydney, was his rescue, one early morning
years ago, of 37 persons from the flooded Hawkes-
bury River.
Editor Thomson W. Leys, of Auckland
(M.L.) Star, was recently offered a seat in the
Legislative Council, but as one of the comps.,
“ Bill” Jennings, was there before him, Thomson
W. declined the honor,
Patrick O’Leary, the man whose wife
owned the cow that kicked over the lamp that
started the great fire of Chicago, is dead. O’Leary,
his wife, and his wife’s cow were long the hardest-
cursed trio in America.
W. P. Reeves, the Maoriland Minister of
Labor, has written the best part of a History of
Communism and Socialism. He means to com-
plete it as soon as he can get the necessary leisure,
and will publish it in England.
Shirley Baker was succeeded as Tongan
Premier by Basil Thomson, son of the late Arch-
bishop of York. Basil is publishing a book on
Tonga, in which volume he will probably give his
candid opinion on the character of his pre-
decessor.
Dr. Odillo Maher, the well-known Sydney
oculist who was hurt in the Redfern railway
accident, has sufficiently convalesced to return to
his home. His eyesight is happily quite restored,
and his other injuries have left no permanent
ill-effects.
A tale was whispered and scouted some
years ago that a certain Judge was given to seek-
ing clairvoyant aid before summing up for or
against prisoners. And only the other day in a
Sydney court the widow of a J.P. deposed on
..ath, during the hearing of a fortune-telling case,
that her deceased husband constantly sought
occult aid in adjudicating.
WhenNesbit, the Adelaide Q.C.,
was in London recently, a gentle-
man at one of the Bohemian clubs
enquired, “ By-the-bye, old man, what
place is Adelaide the capital of?” And
while Nesbit was trying to swallow his
indignation and speak coherently,
Harold Frederic, the author, chipped
in, “ If it’s a secret, you know, we don’t
wish to press the question.”
A certain Minister who is an
Australian University man has for his
Under-Secretary an English University
man. The Minister recently ran his
blue pencil through and through one of
the U.S.’s minutes, wrote beside the
scored paragraph, “ Put this in Eng-
lish,” and returned the U.S. the paper.
Apropos, a former Sydney Lands Office
Minister once ordered Under-Secretary
Charles Oliver to re-write a minute.
Oliver, however, admitted the superior
comprehension of his chief by non-
compliance with the demand ; he laid
the whole matter before the Minister
to sift and decide. Which the Minister
did. Mr. Oliver was modest for a
week ; he merely submitted every paper
to the superior intelligence of the Min-
ister. That Minister “grafted ;” Oliver
sat still in his room. On the first day
of the second week he was waited on
by the Minister, who humbly said :
“For God’s sake, Mr. Oliver, write
your minutes and I will sign them.”
Mr. Oliver cheerfully complied. From
the day Oliver became U.S. for Lands,
that Dept, was Oliver’s, and every
Minister who took charge of it soon
reconciled himself to the fact. It was
Oliver who changed the dignified title
of Surveyor-General to Chief Surveyor,
and the salary of the post from £l2OO
to £BOO per annum.
Since the Age began to advocate a land-
tax about a bushel of letters arrives each week
addressed, “David Syme, Father of Lies, Mel-
bourne,” and little things like that. The Fat
Man’s objurgation is of the primitive order and
very much lacking in originality.
Robert Louis Stevenson had a birthday
recently, and, according to the Star, he went
without his boots in honour of the event. A good
many Australians seem to be keeping their birth-
days in the same fashion, nowadays ; also some of
them seem to have been born almost all the year
round.
F. C. Gray, the nervous draper who
represents Prahran in Victorian Assembly, never
makes a speech without ringing in the dreary
metaphor about “leaving footprints on the sands of
time.” It is the only figure of speech he has got,
and if it were confiscated he would sink into utter
silence.
R. G. Symonds, who under Captain Light
helped to carry out the survey of Adelaide in
1836, was born on Dec. 23, 1810. He still lives in
the city whose cradle he assisted to rock, but he
is growing very feeble. He is as poor now as one
of Douglas Sladen’s “ poems,” and almost as little
remembered.
An old Yass (N.S.W.) identity passed
away last week in the person of “ Mick ” Cassidy,
a mounted policeman of 60 years ago. In 1838
he was wounded by a bushranger with whom he
got into close quarters, and was rewarded by the
Imperial Govt, with a pension. Pensioners are a
long-lived race.
A reporter was taken on the staff of a
metropolitan newspaper lately on the express
condition that while representing the paper he
would make no speeches on public affairs. After
a short trial the temptation to howl mastered his
desire for the “ready,” and the same old orator
harangues the army of the night.
In an Adelaide bookseller’s shop is a draw-
ing by “Mortimer Menpes, 1868” —a pencil
sketch of an English oak, a very mediocre produc-
tion; but Menpes must have been a mere boy then.
There is an Adelaide man, by the way, who re-
members with pride giving office-boy Mortimer
clip on the ear for caricaturing the boss.
It was seriously rumoured that Carington
would get the order of K.G. in succession to the
late Earl Grey. At latest dates Lord Dufferin
was said to be the coming man for this appalling
honor, but evidently Carington’s leg will wear a
Garter before he dies. The historic yawner is
even likely to achieve a dukedom at last by means
of his genius for doing nothing in a cheerful,
courtly way.
When Hori Grey visited a certain Maori-
land town, some 15 years ago, he held a korero
with the Maoris of the district. On the chief
being presented to Grey, a fussy little local trades-
man, with a smattering of the native language,
officiously bustled to the front and acted as inter-
preter, and Grey, who knows Maori better, per-
haps, than any other white man in the province,
with his usual calm courtesy, let him interpret to
his heart’s content, and never even smiled.
J. H. Angas, the S.A. millionaire squatter
and philanthropist, has stopped his subscription
to Adelaide Hospital on the ground that “ it is a
Government institution,” and he has to get square
with the Government somehow for imposing the
new land-tax. Angas has just recovered from a
long and painful shearing, in the course of which
his flocks have been much mangled by cheap
blackleg labor ; and he is probably rather more in-
furiated than usual in consequence. It doesn’t
take much to infuriate the average wealthy philan-
thropist.

ERNEST HOSKING, Conjuror, Exposer of
Spiritualism, and Spook-manufacturer.
For the most workmanlike recent suicide,
the case of William Kirkwood, at Mount Morgan
(Q.), can be commended. He laid himself on a
circular saw, which cut off his head.
W. F. Molesworth, admitted as a barrister
and solicitor by Melbourne Supreme Court last
week, is son of Judge Hickman Molesworth, of
the Insolvency Court, and grandson of old “Billy
Rutledge,” of Warrnambool, one of the early
settlers. This youngest Molesworth represents a
third generation of the family who have practised
at the Victorian bar.

Ihe ennobbled John o. D. Thompson,
Premier of Canada, completed a consistent and
brilliant record of self-advertising by dropping
down dead at Queen Victoria’s luncheon-table.
The thing was unique. No groveller ever thought
of that great yet simple idea before.

J. J. KELLY, the coming wicketkeeper of Australia.
Jules Joubert, they say, hasn’t found his
Hobart Exhibition billet a bed of roses, thus far.
One night he was called to reprove a truculent in-
dividual who wanted to enter the building with a
lighted cigar in his mouth. The man flung his
hot weed at Joubert’s nose and a personal conflict
ensued. The tide of battle carried the energetic
Frenchman, who is not young any more, clean off
his official legs.
A New South Wales J.P., of large girth
and impressive presence, carries Court ethics into
the bosom of his family. Erring domestics are
brought before him, and evidence is taken, and
fines or deprivation of luxuries allotted in due
order. His wife committed flagrant contempt of
this court lately, in refusing to be tried before the
assembled household for an unseemly altercation
with the cook. The contempt has yet to be
purged.
The discussion re the authenticity of Fitz-
Gibbon’s claim to be the descendant of the original
White Knight of Kerry is gravely continued in
Melb. Age, and is very sickenin’ indeed. It does
not seem to have occurred to David Syme that
not one in 5000 of his readers cares a copper about
the descent of the stupendous egotist of the Met-
ropolitan Board. It would be interesting to trace
Fitz back to the original Gibbon, but no one
worries about the White Knight of Kerry.
Dear Bulletin, —Hutton’s “Irish-rabble”
episode brings to memory a somewhat similar inci-
dent many years ago. When Anderson (son of
the Norfolk Island Commandant of the thirties)
bossed the “forces” in Victoria, he had occa-
sion to address the “troops” at a birthday review,
or some such function. The Franco-Prussian war
was in full blast, France being, more or less, under-
neath. Anderson, in praising his amateur soldiery,
recited the old, old wheeze that “ one Englishman
was worth three Frenchmen.” The beastly re-
porters got hold of it, and the newspapers belched
forth at the colonel. Also, almost every French-
man in Victoria, including the consul, challenged
him to fight with all manner of weapons. Ander-
son didn’t fight—in fact, he was scarcely visible
for some weeks while the riot was at its worst— but
he was a much more peaceful colonel thereafter.
—Yours, .
John Thompson, the Canadian Premier,
died last week of syncope, the result of being
invited to Windsor Castle to dine with his
Sovereign Lady. If Dibbs had been troubled
with a weak heart he would have expired sud-
denly under similar circumstances three years
ago. It is said to be a terrible ordeal for one who
doesn’t know the etiquette of Courts —this “com-
mand ” to “ dine and sleep” at Windsor Castle.
Nobody makes the stranger welcome or tells him
just what he ought to do when he gets there. He
is carried away to a bedroom by supercilious
flunkeys, and when he ventures downstairs he is in
mortal terror of meeting the Queen in some corri-
dor, where he ought not to stray, before she has
given him a formal audience. The preliminary
horrors of this visit broke up John Thompson’s
constitution, and during luncheon he collapsed.
They gave him a stiff dose of brandy, “which
somewhat revived him.” But shortly afterwards
the unfortunate politician breathed his last. He
didn’t wait for dinner, but it made little differ-
ence ; the dinner would inevitably have proved
fatal, anyhow. The verdict is that John Thomp-
son, K.C.M.G., died of pomps and vanities, and
Victoria Regina ought to be more careful for the
future.
Schey, M.P., is not the only man who will
be glad to see the last of N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioner Eddy. Not long ago a Minister sent to
him a son of one of his constituents with a most
fulsome letter of recommendation for a billet in
the railway department. The Chief Commissioner
read the letter, and, after asking the boy some-
thing equivalent to how many beans make five,
and getting “four’’for an answer, he turned up
the corner of the letter and wrote : “Seeing that
Mr. So-and-so would be such a desirable addition
to the public service, perhaps the Hon. Blank
could find room for him in his own department.”
Objection was made by a section
of that close guild, the Law, against
the admission of George Ash, M.P.
(S. A.) on the ground that while serving
his articles he worked and earned
money as a member of Parliament. An
incipient barrister, for a certain number
of years before he is called to the bar,
is supposed to do nothing save live on
his property and think over the
statutes, after the manner of those
philosophers who accumulated wisdom
by gazing hard at their own stomachs.
If he is found earning his living by
any honest occupation it is absolutely
fatal to his legal prospects. The Court
ruled, however, that an M.P. doesn’t
“earn” money; he only receives it.
So Ash got through.
Dunedin (M.L.) has lost one of
its old landmarks. “Holy John’’
has pegged out. “Holy John,” an
angular, wild-eyed person in old boots
and ancient black clothes, was the
Jonah of the dreary little city, and for
19 years or more he wandered up and
down the streets calling out occasion-
ally in a cracked voice that the day
of judgment was at hand. Some of
John’s most impressive homilies were
delivered while he was spinning three
feet high in the air and clapping his
heels together, while the dilapidated
tails of his long black coat flew out
behind him like pennons. He gener-
ally carried a large, muddy stick, which
he shook impressively at unbelievers.
No one knew exactly how he lived, but
every day, in rain or snow or shine, he
fled up Pnnees-street telling people as
he passed that they were lost, and
fled down again an hour or two later
stating that the day of resurrection
had come. “Holy John” was one of
the institutions of the city, and Dunedin
will seem lonely without him. He
died of no defined complaint; probably
he ran himself to death in his daily
pilgrimage.
Frank Bowerman, who suicided a few
days ago in Sydney Botanic Gardens by taking a
variety of poisons, was, 20 or 30 years ago, O.P.S.
at Dalby (Q.). He had a misguided ambition to
be a police-magistrate, and, failing to secure the
appointment, he went to Brisbane and attacked
the Under 001.-Secretary, Manning, in his
own office with an axe. Manning was supposed
to be permanently disabled by his injuries, and
by a special Act of Parliament, rushed through
by the Mackenzie Mini-try, he was pensioned off
with <£ooo a year, which liberal allowance he has
enjoyed in good health and spirits for 25 years,
and seems likely to enjoy for many years to come.
Bowerman was sentenced to imprisonment for
life, but was released, mainly through the un-
wearied efforts of his wife, after 12 years’ confine-
ment. The devoted lady died a few hours before
her husband’s discharge from prison, and Bower-
man, an elderly and broken man, thereafter made
a precarious livelihood at a variety of pursuits
until his death. According to a letter which he
left behind him, he made two unsuccessful
attempts to commit suicide with prussic acid.
His last effort was with strychnine and atropine,
and he had four other poisons in his possession,
evidently that he might fall back on them in case
of necessity. A man who couldn’t shuffle out of
life with six bottles of poison would indeed be
unlucky.
ALFRED GRENON, sentenced to death at
Sydney for shooting Thomas Heavey.
The new Czar of Russia, says London
Figaro, “is little in every sense of the word. He
is consumed with arrogance and conceit, and in
character is the most weak-kneed, vacillating,
slack-baked scion that the house of Romanoff has
produced. During his tour in India, the insuffer-
able hauteur which he assumed towards the nobles
who formed his retinue was everywhere remarked
upon. These nobles are men who may one day
drag him off his throne.” Meanwhile, the poten-
tate stands a good chance of getting blown off his
throne.


ADVICE TO PARENTS.
Ip your son in his tender years evinces a desire to
smoke, and thus risks injuring his health, it may
not always be wise to thrash him till he makes up
his mind to stop. In most cases an excellent
remedy is an old pipe soured by long experience
and administered without pity but very strong
tobacco. Above all things avoid letting him ever
get a taste of Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, for then
he will never, never give it up. Keep him for ever
in ignorance of the glorious fact that for a shilling
he may get every wh re a packet of eight of these
mild and aromatic cigars.

Premier Reid is rapidly becoming an
accomplished politician. He can now trust him-
self to be eloquent for two hours without saying
anything.
It is whispered that an attempt will shortly
be made to displace Dibbs from the leadership of
N.S.W. Opposition. Lyne is mentioned as the
coming man.
There is much friction between Trenwith
and Hancock in Vic. Assembly. Not unexpected.
“Whene’er two lions roar along the shore, the
second lion votes the first a bore.”

for land and income taxes; E. W. O’Sullivan
speaking and voting against them.

considered.”

£l5O. There was a memorable majority of 32

M‘Dougal, a railing freetrader and a boss
of the Victorian Tariff Reform League, tells the
Tariff Board that “to reduce the 80 per cent,
duty on strawboard would be to close up the
factory of my firm.” Principle and interest!
Those two eminent statesmen, A. Cameron
and H. Parkes, nearly always sit together in
N.S.W. Assembly—H. Parkes with arms across
chest, head thrown back and inclined to one side
—a monument of disdainful repose ! A. Cameron
ditto, ditto, but with head inclined to the other
side, his face wearing a fine old Roman senatorial
scowl. They present a most careful study in
attitude, and the effect is all that could be desired.

undertakers in Adelaide Muni-
cipal Council, and at a recent
election a monumental mason
(Darby) was elected at the head
of the poll.
Says the Schoolmaster
(London) “To educate a
child for a year in a London
Board school costs <£3 10s. ; to
maintain a ‘wastrel’ in a work-
house for a like period costs
i) 15; while the charge per
annum for a prisoner in the
county gaol is .£24. In 1870,
when the Education Act was
passed, there were 30,000 per-
sons in gaols and prisons in
England and Wales. In 1890,
with an increase in population
of four millions, the number
had fallen to 19,000. In 1870
there were 113 convict estab-
lishments to be maintained.
In 1892 practically one-half the
number, 57. Those Australian
politicians who want to econo-
mise by cutting down the
Education vote should think
hard over these figures. The
money that is saved off educa-
tion would probably have to
go in building gaols.
President Carnot’s death
was the cause of a succession
of tragedies. A workman who
was putting up the funeral
hangings at the presidential
palace fell off a ladder and
broke his neck. One of Car-
not’s servants died of apoplexy
brought on by excitement and
grief. A woman went mad
on hearing of the crime and
killed herself by jumping out
of a top window. There were
four fatal accidents in the crush
at the funeral. A school-
master, who was endeavouring
to demonstrate the exact man-
ner in which the murder was
committed, did it so realisti-
cally that be killed one of his
audience with a table-knif' 1 .
The Belgian Minister at Pans
died of excitement. For a
fortnight after the assassina-
tion the suicide rate trebled
all over France, and ten people
were either killed or badly
injured in free fights arising
out of discussions upon Anar-
chism.

Britain is alleged to he about to annex
Ashantee. The Ashantees defeated Sir Charles
McCarthy’s army in 1825 and hung the dead
general’s skull up in the fetish house at Coomassie*
and Britain has never quite got level for that
insult to this day.

Melville, M.L.C. (Vic.), wants to insert
an amendment in the Barristers and Solicitors
Bill, providing that women be admitted as legal
practitioners. The only logical objection is that
when the lady solicitor embezzles the trust money
she can’t very well be a “ gent one.”

The self-created N.S.W. “Retrenchment’’
party, aggrieved at what it considers to be the
ingratitude of ex-Premier Dibbs, is intriguing to
depose that politician from the leadership of the
N.S.W. Opposition. One mad section has actu-

bull-post.
Ash, M.P., pointed out in the S.A.
Assembly, the other day, that the Bill which has
for one of its objects the licensing of foster-
mothers and nurses to destitute children, is wide
enough in its provisions to include the she-wolf
that suckled Romulus and Remus. It alludes to
“ any female.”
Maoriland, which for some months past
had been losing population by emigration to
Australia, has now stopped the leak, and the
November returns show a large balance of arrivals
over departures. A very large proportion of the
arrivals are Maorilanders coming back “ stone-
broke” from Westralia.

blatant animal at the next S.A. Parliamentary
elections, when proputty will have no say.

Parker, the recently-retired Col Sec-
retary of W.A., complained that Premier Forrest
treated his colleagues as subordinates. Why not.
Under the system of Party Government what are
the colleagues but subordinates ? If a. PrBl P®*
resigns, he ends the official existence of the whole
team. If one of the team resigns, does it move
the Premier? Not a bit of it.

Once, when Sammy Raphael was speaking
before the ratepayers of Adelaide, a Register
reporter made him say, in print, that he was m
favor of a tax on dogs and buxom witches, hammy
said “ buckskin breeches.” A Hansard reporter

Thomas Elder, who has been confined to
his room for some months, was driven into Ade-
laide on Dec. 1 to record a plurality of votes
against the acquirement by the Corporation of an
electric-lighting plant for the city. The gas mono-
poly won because many of its supporters had a
property vote in each of the six wards, whereas
the members of the other party, as a rule, could
only vote in the ward in which they reside.

In receiving a Sydney unemployed depu-
tation, the other day, N.S.W. Premier Reid said
he would find bread and, if possible, work for the
needy, but he would have nothing to do with
“ professional agitators.” Now, Premier Reid is
himself a barrister—a man who agitates your ease,
or that of any other man, for a fee. If he is not a pro-
fessional agitator, what is he? The leading
difference between professional-agitator Reid and
professional-agitator Tompkins, of “the Statue,”
is that Reid belongs to a strongly-entrenched
trades-union and Tompkins doesn’t.
N.S.W. Premier Reid’s logic is unanswer-
able that there can be no conference between the
Legislative Assembly and the Council concerning
the rights of either body. If the Assembly, upon
the Council’s declaration that it possessed the
power to amend money-bills, yielded a conference
in order to debate that point, it would virtually
concede the whole contention. Provided the
Assembly is going to stick to its point—as it must
do if the land and income-taxes are ever to become
| aw —then there is nothing to confer about. The
whole matter is already decided so far as the
Assembly is concerned. If it confers it gives its
case away.
A Melbourne firm was recently convicted
of branding its inferior guns with the name of
a famous maker, and escaped with a nominal
punishment. Being asked in Parliament if any
further action was to be taken in the matter,
Premier Turner replied that he “was placed
rather awkward position, because the firm in
question were clients of his in the action brought
against them by the gun-makers whose name was
improperly used.” Thus Turner in his private
capacity defends the action in which the Govern-
ment —which is mainly Turner in his public
capacity—is the plaintiff. Consequently, both
sides of the case are Turner. Evidently Turner
is a lawyer first and then a premier, and his
“ awkward position ” is nothing to that of the
country whose prime minister’s public actions are
hobbled by his private interests.

for piles is most remarkable, large quantities
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-street, Sydney.

A N.S. Wales doctor carried his made-out
bill to the bedside of a dying patient, the other
day. The patient, a woman, lived only a few
minutes after seeing the document, and he
•announced her death and presented his bill to the
husband at the same time. The husband paid the
account, took a receipt and then—well, the doctor
was the younger man and was able to jump the
atairs, thus getting an advantage at the start of
the race.

ment in aid of the Ballarat Hospital, “owing to the
'Unsympathetic criticisms which have appeared in the
'newspapers.”
The Fitzroy (Melbourne) City Council,
after an absence of 20 years, has been giving per-
formances again. In ’67, the “scenes” in the
Council used to draw like the nigger minstrel
shows. All the larrikins out of work would roll
up to see the fun on Council night. One night a
seven-foot councillor laid the Mayor on the floor,
put “ the chair ” on top of him, and sat in it. His
Washup was so completely flattened out that he
was never any shape worth mentioning afterwards.

Hobart Exhibition proved a wet place for
a certain “Commission” representing an Aus-
tralian province. The commissioners managed to
get through about 35 cases of champagne in the
first four days.
By the way, one official personage sailed for
Hobart with a couple of beauteous ladies in tow,
but on the Exhibition opening night he was
shocked by the news that one of the sultanas had
thrown off her allegiance and disappeared. Then
ensued a wild, unsober hunt for the missing lady
by the bereaved gentleman and a number of in-
fluential friends. The members of the search-
party had strict injunctions to seize the stray
charmer by fair means or foul, and cart her back
to the “protector’s” hotel. The adventurers
returned in due course, bringing each of them a
female ruin in the last stage of dishevelment, who
had willingly pleaded guilty to being the lost
Venus. Six or more grinning harridans arrived
at the hotel in cabs, heralded by cries of “ I’ve
found her, dear boy,” from the captors. When
they had each in turn been disowned by the chief
of the expedition, they joined forces and began to
“ play up ”on the hotel steps. Finally the land-
lord turned a hose upon the struggling mass of
distinguished visitors, and peace was at length
restored. The fate of the nymph that escaped is
still shrouded in doubt.
• # *
Cardinal Moran, when he ridicules Win-
deyer and Co. for guaranteering the authenticity
of Mrs. Spook Mellon, touches a subject that a
wiser Cardinal would have let alone. The same
remark applies to every other parson who takes a
hand in the controversy. Mrs. Mellon lives on
the ghosts of Cissie and Josephine. The Church
lives on the ghosts of Samuel, Lazarus and others.
The Mellon female attests that the alleged ghost
which you can see is genuine ; the Church attests
that the alleged ghost which some unknown
writer tells ns that Saul was told the witch of
Endor saw was also genuine. The dubious spectre
of 2700 years ago has no right to throw stones at
the dubious spectre of to-day.

An Auckland (M.L.) corporation laborer
drowned himself instead of appearing before a
magistrate in answer to a charge of having
assaulted his wife, “ with whom he had lived un-
happily ever since he made his property over to
her,” says the unconscious pathos of the local
paper. Why don’t people read “King Lear?”
Well-digested Shakespeare is the best recipe for a
happy life.

Another ghostly specimen of literature
from the Melbourne undertakers’ organ, In
Memokiam :
The funeral of Annie , aged eight and half years
consisting of glass Hearse, and white plumes, and one
mourning coach, a very profuse display of flowers and
followed by several vehicles, private and hired ; moved
from the residence of Mr. —— Winfield in Gwalter
Terrace, Park Road, Brunswick proceeding first to the
Church of England in Glenlyon Road East, where a por-
tion of the burial service was performed by the Rev. C.
M. Bardine, the choir rendering their portion of the ser-
vice in an especially meritorious manner. After the
service the Cortege re-formed and now headed by the
Sunday-school fellows and friends of the deceased little
one; proceeded along the Sydney Road, until the
Cemetery was reached where they opened out in proper
military style, allowing the Funeral to pass through, and
then following on. The Rev. Mr. Bardine now leading to
the grave-side where the burial service was completed,
and after the choir and children had sung “There’s a
home for little Children,” the grave was filled. The
ranks of scholars then re-formed for the return march,
and all was over. The display of floral offerings was very
large ; and the coffin which was covered with figured
plush, and silver lace, and mountings, called forth especial
comment.

The chief items of interest at Alfred
Stephen’s sale of household effects were a “squat”
tumbler 100 years old and an armchair, formerly
the property of Governor Gipps. The portrait of
the ex-Chief Justice by Thomas Griffiths Wain-
wright didn’t appear.
* * *
A wholesale surprise was occasioned last
week by the remarkable poverty of the late Alfred
Stephen’s much-advertised library, and consider-
able disgust was excited among buyers when it
became known that, after the announcement of
the sale, the leading feature of the library—the
ex-Chief Justice’s large collection of rare Austra-
lian pamphlets—had been privately sold for £75.
The executors, no doubt, thought they were
making a good bargain, but, as it happens, six of
the works sold would easily bring, in the open
market, £5 to =£lo apiece.

All the surviving sufferers by the Redfern

* * •
About the funniest tug-of-war on record
was between Chinese and Hindoos, on the closing
night of the St. Patrick’s Gamble at Melb. Exhibi-
tion. At the start a Chinese-cwm-Hindoo equiva-
lent for a ringing cheer went up, and then
Mongolians and Hindoos poured into the ring,
unable to contain themselves as quiet spectators.
The suave smiling Chows skipped about like mad
things fanning individual members of their team
with handkerchiefs, hats, sashes, coats, and other
apparel. They danced around in their puffed-
out pantaloons, jumping spasmodically up and
down, sideways, here and there and everywhere,
with their arms, legs, heads, little round bodies
and baggy breeches and sleeves going in all direc-
tions. Grotesque-looking Chinamen, well up in
years, leaped high into the air in their crazy excite-
ment, yelling, shrieking, and bellowing all the
while, and came down most unholy croppers,
often on bundles of live crackers. Then an Indian
would fall over the prostrate Chow, be sworn at,
and reciprocate. Three or four of both tribes
would join in the argument, and start scruffing
each other. Next, all the Celestials in the shindy
but one would look round, see their team gain an
inch or two of the rope, and, with a joyous shout,
rush off waving their arms and legs frantically,
and try to fan somebody, it didn’t matter whom,
and dance about and scream. The remaining
Chow was forthwith set on by the other four
Indians and pummelled. Then a Chinkie, who
had run away, would suddenly recollect that the
fight had to be attended to, single out the first
Hindoo he saw, walk up and hit him. Thus fight
followed fight, yet they didn’t seem to mean any
harm, and no accidents happened. Every now
and then, two or three policemen would collar a
frenzied Chow, and eject him over the ropes, but
a dozen rushed to fill the breach, and the
confusion was intensified. The Indians were
getting beaten. With the national honor in
jeopardy, several of their barrackers resolved on
heroic remedies, and earnestly laid into the whole
team right and left, with ugly-looking sticks. It
was suggestive of a rowed-out eight being pitched
into with a stock-whip by the cox. to make them
quicken up a trifle. However, it was no go, and
the Indians were gradually hauled over. Then
the fun began again. Crowds of Mongolians
hoisted their men shoulder high, and cantered
excitedly round the grounds, while others rushed
wildly all over the place in ineffable ecstacy,
shaking hands with all and sundry. Groups of
humorous Caucasian youth took to raising on their
shoulders every Chow they could catch and rush-
ing him into the refreshment - room or into
Nicholson-street, or the Lord only knows where.
Meanwhile the Indians cursed at large, but no
one heeded them. When they got in the road
the victorious Chinamen and their barrackers
simply rushed over them, and at last they gathered
themselves together and sneaked sorrowfully
away. #

with him for 13 years?” “Because he followed
us about, and was always anxious to have my
children vaccinated.”

* • •
The Bulletin respectfully suggests to
Messrs. Windeyer and Co. that the best way to
test Mrs. Mellon would be to lock up the lady in
a large iron safe, with one small hole in the top
for ventilation—said safe being lent for the occa-
sion by some well-known dealer, who will receive
a handsome bonus if the spirits don’t get out.
Then put a cordon of detectives round the safe,
deposit conveniently a large dish of boiling water
for the apparition to step into, and wait results.
Justice Hodges, reversing the Fitts decree
nisi in Melb. Divorce Court: “ I cannot hope that
these persons will ever live together, or that the
marriage will be any good to themselves or society.
But I am unwillingly driven to the conclusion
that I must allow the bond to stand.” The
Church and the Law agree on one matrimonial
point. When there is very little chance of the
parties living together any more the bond must
not be broken.
• • •
One of the earliest causes of suspicion
against Mrs. Mellon, the Sydney spookist, was
the constant and unaccountable variation in the
dimensions of “Cissy.” “Cissy” was of many
different heights, and her total variation was
something like two feet. Of course no one can
assert definitely that a ghost has any exact
stature, but the general impression was that
“Cissy” carried her want of any definite resem-
blance to herself altogether too far. Another
complaint was that Mrs. Mellon and “ Geordie ”
would never speak simultaneously. Unless some
method can be discovered by which a medium can
talk twice at the same time, this discrepancy
seems incurable.

MY “BULLETIN.”
Torn and ragged the outside edges,
Stained the pages with mould and damp.
“ Has the bag rolled off in the muddy sedges
That border the river ’tween town and camp?”
No. On the cover of faded red,
“Ex Wairarapa,” the writing said.
Gisborne, M.L.

At the bottom are the request and grant as follows:
“Most Holy Father, Mr. T. J. Byrnes, M.L.C., LL.D.,
8.A., humbly prostrate at the feet of Your Holiness, begs
you to grant to him for himself, and his near relatives to
the third degree, the Apostolic Benediction and Plenary
Indulgence in articulo mortis in the usual form of the
Church, and prescribed by the Apostolic See.—Our Most
Holy Lord Pope Leo XIII. kindly consents as a favor.
Given at the Vatican, sth September, 1894. ‘J. Arch-
bishop of Nicomedia.’ ”
Humbly prostrate ! Isn’t it about time to give
up the horizontal variety of religion—the kind
that has to be taken lying flat on the stomach
with one’s, nose in the dust —and to find some
creed which admits of a vertical attitude?

• • •
Why is it that when a parson, like
Garnsey, drops dead in his harness, all the world
wonders? The common cart-horses of commerce
and the bony mill-horses of labor drop dead daily
and hourly, and a busy world does not so much
as lift its head from its ledger to see what the row
is about.
Within one week recently six cases of sui-
cide were reported in various parts of Australia,
in each of which the leading character got up
early in the morning and disposed of himself.
Also, a Maoriland woman, suffering from tem-
porary derangement, got up early and threw her-
self over a cliff 250 feet high, and escaped almost
uninjured. One of the first really sensational
suicides in Victoria, by the way, was that of an
itinerant preacher who got up at 4 o’clock on the
morning after his marriage, and nearly hacked
his head off with a blunt saw. This tendency on
the part of the suicide to get up early—or, rather,
the tendency on the part of people who get up
early to commit suicide—is a matter which should
lie scientifically investigated.
# * •
The Church of England self-denial week,
in Victoria, has raised about half Bishop Goe’s
annual screw.
# # *
A Maoriland Wesleyan authority writes
to the papers to explain away the church’s alleged
greed and inhumanity in the case of old Probert,
the recent suicide. John Probert, he points out,
left his fortune, amounting to .£7OO or £lBOO a
year, in trust to the Wesleyan Church, to be used
for certain sectarian purposes, and, therefore, even
though the sect knew that its benefactor’s brother
was old and poor and infirm, it couldn’t legally
make him even the smallest allowance out of the
fund. Which is doubtless true, and would be
sufficiently convincing but for the obvious fact
that the church could have made him a small
allowance out of some other fund if it felt that
way inclined. The apology is only another evi-
dence that explanation is one of the fine arts which
few men ever acquire.

The famous “prophet” who foretold the
destruction of Maoriland by earthquake before the
end of 1894, and who induced a large number of
people including* two solicitors, sundry Civil
servants, and many old women with property—to
flee from the trouble to come, has been heard of
from South Africa. According to the latest in-
telligence, someone accuses the seer of steal-
ing <£2ooo from one of his old lady emigrants,
borne of his followers are lost in the interior
of Africa, a lot more are camping on the deso-
late highlands of Damascus, and some are pain-
fully working their passages back to Maoriland.
Yet the next prophet will boom just the same.
Ihe prophetic business is immortal.
• * *
One energetic advertiser among the Waira-
rapa passengers had himself photographed in his
alleged costume as be came ashore from the wreck,
with a life-belt in his hand, a tattered pair of
pyjamas for his sole attire, and the heaving sea for
a background. As the camera fiend didn’t arrive
till a day or two after the mishap, it is naturally
inferred that the passenger undressed himself and
re-assumed his shipwrecked attitude expressly for
photographic purposes. And, in so doing, he was
wise in his generation, for a judicious distribution
of his photo, as he came ashore in his airy costume
will help materially to boom his business, whatever
it may be. It doesn’t matter whether advertising
is relevant or not, so long as it is vigorous and
original. If Farkes could swim ashore from a
wreck and be photographed while still wet on the
beach, and the picture appeared in all the papers,
he might be Premier of N.S. Wales again inside
of a year.

A Bourke-street (Melb.) chemist displays
a complete male skeleton in his window, and all
the advanced women of the city congregate out-
side. There is a certain indefinable boniness of
soul about the advanced woman, and she naturally
gravitates towards skeletons.

(Vic.) tragedy: “She rose early this morning,
attended to certain household duties, milked the
cow, then cut her child’s throat with a butcher’s
knife, and immediately afterwards hanged herself
to a rafter.” The fatal consequences of early-
rising onee more.

* « «
A Narandera (N.S.W.) magistrate has
ukased that half-made brews of beer shall cease
to ferment on Sunday, in honor of the sacred
memory of Charles 11., late of Hingland. Now,
will some magistrate, acccording to the same old
Sabbath law of Charles Stuart, decree that interest
on P.N.’s and overdrafts and mortgages and bills
of sale shall not accumulate on Sunday? The
usurer, when he charges interest on Sunday,
follows his worldly calling for purposes of gain,
which is distinctly illegal.
* # #
The sleepiest man in Australia was fined
205., a few days back. He was discovered dream-
ing on the railway line, and explained that he had
put his ear on the rail to find out if the train
was coming, and had dozed off.
# « «
Dear Bulletin, — When will the theory be
exploded that women are the gentler sex ? Going
up a steep incline on a heavily-loaded Sydney
’bus, the vehicle was stopped four times in the
space of 10 yards by women getting out and in.
The street was slippery from recent watering, and
the frantic straining, slipping and wrenching of
the poor horses in starting each time would have
moved a blackfellow, but tender-hearted woman
took no notice. I saw a party of them fooled by
the cable tram on King-street. They wanted to
stop it on the incline, but as the strain is on the
machinery, and not on flesh and blood, the grip-
man did not pull up, but waved them to the
corner. They would not walk 20 yards, but
waited and stopped the next ’bus, and the horses
nearly tore themselves asunder in making a fresh
start. —P.A.

Respondent in the very latest aristocratic

indeed.
* * •
On one of the State village settlements in
Victoria, the clean men adopt an original method
for making the dirty ones wash themselves. They
“ ringbark ” the unwashed ones by drawing a
chalk-line round them, and refuse to allow them
to come outside until they have taken a bath, a
bucket of water and a cake of soap being placed
inside the ring.

A coincidence: In Australia the same
papers which contained the news of the assassina-
tion of the Emperor Alexander 11. of Russia, also
announced the loss of the U.S.S- Oo.’s Tara r~~
with 112 1 ives. Thirteen years afterwards the
same papers which published the death of Czar
Alexander 111. also had the news of the loss of the
Union Oo.’s Wairarapa, with 127 lives. This
Rives Australia personal reason to hope the
Nihilists will give Nicholas 11. a little more rope.

“ IMITATION
is the
SINOEREST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as ■ regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet in face of all they seem to thrive, and
the paper which hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks cham-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped to
deception and m if representation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to
their value can be found in every district, and
such being the case it is unnecessary to further
extol their virtues.
A SONG OF THE PRESENT.
“Frivolity, frivolity !’’
Alas, where is the jollity
That used to be
In times when we
Could sing and dance and laugh and flirt,
And none could see where was the hurt ?
“Frivolity !” is all the cry,
And “ Girls are naught but vanity !”
So lie the good !
However could
The little dears be such a thing?
Let’s love them still! Life’s on the wing!
Let’s take what joy the world now has !
Let vanitatum vanitas
Go to the wind 1
Though preachers find
That all the world has since gone wrong,
Live j love ! Like this, life’s but a song !

What evil genuis presides over a Privy
Counoillorship? Consider the celerity with which
“colonial ” politicians “ pass in their cheques ” as
soon as they have been created P.C.’s! Dailey,
Stephen, Macdonald, of Canada, and now
Thompson, also of Canada, all betook themselves
from the mundane sphere almost immediately
after having been so “ honored.” Apparently
P.C. is an incurable disease.
To meet an, insistent demand that this
characteristic Australian book should be procur-
able in more permanent form, “A Golden
Shanty,” by Bulletin writers, is now supplied in
neat cloth binding at 25., post free to any address.
It contains some of the best and most original
work which has helped to make The BULLETIN
famous, and no Australian library is complete
without it.
Dr. Garran, who loses a salary of £695 a
year for doing practically nothing, thinks that
the action of the retrenchment party in squelch-
ing the useless N.S.W. Conciliation Board was a
mistake. Since the Board has decided exactly
2 cases in exactly 2£ years —or at the rate of about
£2500 a case—most people, who do not get £695 a
year for doing practically nothing, will disagree
vehemently with Dr. Garran. When Govern-
ment establishes the new system of compulsory
arbitration the expenses can easily be started
again. There is never any trouble in starting
expenses. In the meantime, the province is too
young—or too old—to pay for sinecures.
Ferdinand de Lesseps, the originator of
the two biggest swindles of the century, has died
at the age of 89. For cold-blooded infamy, the
stories of the Suez and Panama Canal schemes
have no rival. The Suez fraud was, if possible,
the more utterly heartless of the two, but there
was very little indeed to choose between them,
save in the fact that one succeeded and the
other didn’t. De Lesseps was not the “engineer
of the Suez Canal,” nor of anything else.
“I don’t know why people persist in calling
me an engineer, when I am nothing of the
kind,” he said once, “except, perhaps, in the
sense of engineering the engineers.” De Lesseps
was a bad financier and a second-rate diplomatist,
but he had much back-stairs influence with Napo-
leon 111., and Napoleon put the screw on the
Khedive so hard that that miserable Egyptian
was ultimately compelled to pay almost the whole
coat of building the canal out of his own purse,
while the ostensible capital of the Canal Co. was
spent in champagne and fireworks. The Panama
scheme failed because in that case there was no
Khedive to be looted and no Napoleon to put the
screw on.
At Poyerty Point.
A realistic chorus representing broken-down land-
boomers and depositors would be a novel feature for a
Melb. Xmas pantomime this year. Consider them in all
their glory—the men from the doss-house, and the Yarra
bank, and the gaol, and the Benevolent Asylum, and the
mad-house ; the dirty men, the unkempt men, the shabby-
genteel men, the ravenous, wolfish-looking men, the men
with grass-seeds in their clothes and their hair standing
up through their battered hats, the unshod men and the
men with broken boots, and the men gnawing crusts and
ham-bones, the tattered women, the children, all doing a
species of dance of death behind the footlights. The
manager who put that spectacle in the middle of a
glittering panto, would either make his fortune or be
secretly sandbagged in the street— The Bulletin doesn’t
profess to know which.
The best “all round” actress was on the wharf. A
countrywoman bumped her, and, says the all-round one,
“Are you going to walk over me? ” “Begorra, I’d sooner
walk over ye than round ye.”
A Sydney man who paid £4 to see some of Mrs. Mellon’s
so-called materialising stances, now writes that he must
have his money back or “know the reason why.” If
Mrs. Mellon used to charge .1.4 a head for admission, she
must have made a reasonable pile out of “ Geordie ” and
the rest.
The brassy voice of Mr. Roberts is probably the most
original feature of the Firm’s unfortunate comedy co.
When he appeared in the “New Boy,” most people
imagined that his galvanic manner, and part, at least, of
the brassiness of his vocal organ were assumed for the
character, but later experience showed that the owner
was simply built that way.
Maggie Moore’s show at Melbourne Royal is a triumph
of careful management in all respects, and that wonder-
ful woman will “ stand on velvet” when her pantomime
season opens.
“The Gaiety Girl ” is due at Easter with its galaxy of
feminine beauty and the adorable Riley and his legs.
W. and M.’s Comic Opera Co. will be three months in
Maoriland. After that it will probably disband, as the
leading lights—Nellie Stewart, Brownlow, Tapley, Elsie
Cameron, and, very likely, Lauri—are all going ’ome.
The matronly and massive Elsie Cameron, of the
" Firm’s ” Qpmic Opera Co., has dropped into a nice little

Pennyman, Gov. Duff’s confidential clerk, was a com-
mittee-man at Hosking’s spook-seance, on the last night
of the season, at Sydney Opera House. His business was
to retire with the conjuror, and see that he undressed
himself satisfactorily. In five minutes both returned,
Hosking in an inky suit, with the bristles of his hair more
confused than ever. Pennyman approached the floats
and said : “ Ladies and gentlemen, I have examined Mr.
Hosking carefully, and can assure you that he has abso-
lutely nothing upon him.”
The Girl of Yarmouth refused to dance for the benefit
of the Ballarat Hospital because of the tone of a section of
the press in dealing with his charitable efforts at Hobart.
Yarmouth is too sensitive for the ballet.
At least one of the persons at the Wiudeyer spook-
seance (and a signatory, by the way, of the famous certi-
ficate) distinctly declares that while she is unconvinced as
to the spirit-origin of the alleged materialisations, she is,
on the other hand, quite certain that Mrs. Mellon is notan
impostor, and does not cause the “phenomena” by
trickery. “ For,” she says, “ Mrs. Mellon hasn’t got the
brains to be an expert conjuror !”
The librettist of “Predatoros” repudiates The Bulle-
tin’s suggestion that his opera “germinated in the
‘Mountebanks’ and derived a little sustenance from the
‘ Pirates of Penzance,’” and declares that “as a fact it
was fully sketched out and partly written 14 years ago.”
Evidently the low-minded Gilbert has been borrowing
Hart’s ideas in the interim.
Williamson and Musgrove wanted Miss Orelli, the
clever Melb. amateur, for the part of Cinderella in their
Sydney pantomime, but the young singer is not open to
theatrical engagements of any kind just now. There’s a
large future in store for MissO. if she will ordy work and
wait for it. A course of pantomime training would be
the worst possible thing that could happen to a nineteen-
year-old damsel whose health and voice have not quite
recovered from a severe illness.
Alford, the Sydney constable who was
disabled—and, so far as at present appears, per-
manently disabled—by Montgomery, the burglar,
has been pensioned for 12 months at the rate of ss.
per day. After that, if his case proves hopeless,
matters are to be “reconsidered,” which means
that his pension may be continued, or reduced,
or stopped altogether. As Alford’s pay was 7s. 6d.
a day he is thus, for the present, fined by the
State one-third of his income for his services in
arresting two men whom the State decided to be
most dangerous criminals. Considering how the
then Premier, Dibbs, gushed over the “gallant
constables,” the situation is quaint.
The Loch and Ben brand of old Highland
whisky (Leo Reiss and Co., of 492 Kent-street,
Sydney, agents) is a comparatively new variety
which ought to make its way in the Australian
market. It is a mild yet potent liquor with a fine
flavor which seems, in some vague fashion, to
suggest reminiscences of rugged Scotch scenery,
and precipices, and the Children of the Mist, and
other Caledonian facts and legends. Also, it is
old and mature like the land where it has its
origin, and tastes like the bottled atmosphere of
freedom, and it is cheap, which is more important
than even the atmosphere of freedom, in times
like the present.
Parsons’ “Grains of Gold,” one of the
most nutritious foods now on sale, is steadily
creating a demand for itself in Australia. It
differs from many others mainly because it con-
tains all the valuable portions of the grain. These
portions are generally sacrificed for the sake of
color and appearance, and consequently the
ordinary flour of commerce contains little but
starch, its muscle and flesh-forming elements
having been carefully removed and thrown away.
The “ Grains of Gold ” are food prepared on the
logical principle that man eats to live ; ordinary
food is prepared on the illogical idea that he lives
to admire the color and unnatural whiteness of
his victuals.
In N.S.W. Assembly the motion to reduce
Leg. Council Chairman of Committees Jacob’s
screw from .£470 to £2 was defeated by only four
votes. This places the Council’s stipendiary
terrier in a particularly awkward dilemma.
Henceforth he must either cease his antagonism
to the Lower House in general and the Labor-party
in particular—in which case he will probably be
sacked by the Council which only appointed him
on account of his reliable Toryism —or keep on
nagging at the “ members of another place till
the four men who saved him lose their tempers
and his salary is abolished.

Dear Bulletin, —Australians have no room for
sneers at the English cricketers for discriminating between
“ Smith ” and “ Mr. De Vere.” We’re pretty well as bad.
For instance, the principal reason—or so it is alleged
why Billy Hnvell, the Richmond (N.S.W.) farmer, was
not included in the test match was because he is not ‘ a
society man,” and isn’t varnished up to specification!
Ughl — Yours, Jones.

George Giffen's scores against England — sB (not
out) and 64, and against Victoria—94 (not out), or 216 runs
for once out, place him easily at the head of the averages
in Australian cricket. In bowling against England he
took 12 wickets for 219 ; against Victoria, 12 for 140 or
24 wickets for 359 runs.
* » * *
Dear Bulletin, — How is it that the score of
1238 for 9 wickets, obtained by the Ulster O.C. v. Mac-
quarie 0.C., at Moore Park in 1874, is discarded in all
cricket records? There is not the slightest doubt con-
cerning its genuineness. Some cricketers state that the
reason is on account of the match being only of third-rate
order. But no matter what rank it is, does it not remain
as the record score in any match? —Yours, Kaew.

Harry Dyer, who made 102 for S. A. against
Vic., on Adelaide Oval, is a country player who has been
playing for S. Adelaide Club for two years past, and has a
great record. Dyer is a thin, weak-looking cricketer, but
he hits with tremendous power.

The Bulletin deeply regrets that, while under
an unfortunate misapprehension, it should have placed
Humphreys, the English veteran, before the Australian
public in an altogether mistaken light. Humphreys,
it appears, is 45, not 47, years of age, he has but two sons,
whereas this paper credited him with nine, and he owns
in England a very large and eminently successful business.
» * » *
Richardson is very highly thought of by his
brother Englishmen. Bowling his fastest he can make
the hall turn a foot on the Sydney pitch. His first to
Blackham pitched outside the off-stump and broke clean
across, just missing the leg stump. “Ho!” exclaimed
the Australian captain, thankful for the life. Whiz!
came the second, and the great w icket-keeper was bowled
again, but the timbers stood. Then, feeling that Fate
ordained it, Blackham made 74 without giving a chance.
And not until he had made that 74 did Richardson get the
ball past him for the third time and shatter his wicket.
Such is cricket—and such is luck!

London Referee is responsible for the state-
ment that “professional amateurism” in cricket, foot-
ball, &c., was first started by the Australians. “ They
were all mad on cricket. Rivalry between the colonies
was high indeed. Amateurs would not, of course, lake
pay. But the able amateur cricketer found noble induce-
ments to accept some sort of a berth and shift his loca-
tion, or, maybe, he was similarly influenced to remain
where he was. If he could play cricket real well his
ability as a bank-clerk or a Government official, or what
not, was rated far more highly than if he did not possess
that first-named gift.” All of which is true, though it
doesn’t alter the fact that W. G. Grace was spoken
of as a “paid amateur’’—directly or indirectly paid
—several years before Australia developed cricket mad-
ness. Talking of Graces, the elder brother, E.M., was
pitchforked into the billet of county coroner long, long
ago, so that he might be the better able to devote time to
county matches.
♦ * * *
The most objectionable feature of the big
Melbourne Bicycle Meeting was the betting. The
“hoarse-throated” ringmen abounded, and could not be
suppressed. Over a hundred bookmakers were turned
out of the ground, but only to pay another half-crown
and get in again. Oue was taken to the look-up to serve
as a test-case, and Magistrate Panton fined him £5.
With this precedent the M.C.C. holds a weapon which it
intends to wield with effect on future occasions. Mean-
time, the leading cycling authorities show no disposi-
tion to smother the gambling which is threatening to kill
the sport; for a large number of the men, challenged by
the police for shouting the odds, produced in triumph
betting-permits signed by the secretary of the M.B.C. 1
All the scratch men left in the final for the
Austral Wheel Race were obviously handicapped out of a
chance. The little group struggled hard to lessen the
distance that separated it from the main body, but the
effort was futile, and towards the finish they gave up try-
ing. The remarkable thing is that in the heats the
scratch men had no difficulty in overhauling the limit
riders after the third or fourth lap. It is, therefore,
clear that there is a distinct difference in the conditions
of the preliminary arid final heats, and the question
arises : should the competitors left in the final be re-han-
dicapped? There are many arguments in favor. First of
all, the scratch men have to compete against an aggregate
of the best men. This, together with the desire to be
first and not merely amongst the first five, causes the pace
to be swifter, and everything which tends to quicken the
pace is a disadvantage to the scratch men and a corres-
ponding benefit to the other competitors.

Cis Parker, the jockey, was last week carpeted
by the A.J.C. for the misdemeanor of having won a
thousand pound double on the two Cups, Caulfield and
Melbourne. He was let off with a caution not to do it
again, as if any man was likely to spot such a double
twice in a lifetime.
» » * •
The bitter cry of the punter who backed the
Paris and Patron “double,” but didn’t get his money, is
heard in all the highways and byevvays of Smellbourne.
As a rule the defaulting bookmakers scorned to hide
themselves when settling-day arrived. They came up to
the scratch with a careless that they were
stone-broke, and it subsequently transpired that several
of them had been insolvent at the critical moment in
several previous years. This happened to be a year when
a lot of people “struck it,” whereas the “ double "-
finders (who didn’t draw a dividend) in ’93 and ’92 were
so few that the story of their wrongs was not widely
published. “ Laying doubles on the never ” has become
a popular profession down South since the ‘ boom”—
a fact which should bring consolation to many poor
jugginses who nearly pulled off the long odds when
Glenloth or Tarcoola got home. The joy of knowing that
one had a very narrow escape from being swindled is
worth paying ill for. The extra satisfaction of hearing
that a number of luckier punters are now tearing their
hair is thrown in, free of charge.
» • * *
Joe Thompson won over ,£14,000 on the Cam-
bridgeshire. He did not write the name of the winner,
Indian Queen, in any double, and he also backed Tedd v
Hobson’s mare to win outright. His reasons for doing
so, as told in the choicest Thompsonian vernacular,
are most amusing, and would be appreciated by anyone
who is acquainted with the resources of the Australian
language.
