Sculling is played out as a profession. Cham-
pion sculler Stansbury, after having pretty well “done
the globe, vainly inviting people to come on, is now
obliged to look round for some other method of earning a
crust. “No one with any 7 money will row me,” he says.
In America his performances didn’t exceed a few' trifling
exhibitions. He is now settled down on the Parramatta,
where he is principally engaged in growing fat. His row-
ing weight is 12st. 10ib., and his present vast dimensions
are 16 stone.
* * * *
If Steve O’Donnell and Ed. Smith meet in the
States, Smith should win, as he would probably show to >
much “devil” for the scientific Steve. Jim Hall is
anxious for a cut at O Donnell, and, in the writers
opinion, Steve w'ould have a better show with Hall, not-
withstanding the latter’s wonderful science, than with a
rushing fighter like Smith.
Dan Creedon has done himself harm in the
States by not taking his defeat as a man. Creedon’s
statement that Fitzsimmons wins his fights by an unfair
blow with the wrist is bunkum, as to strike such a blow
Fitz would stand a good show of breaking his wrist or
arm. As a matter of fact, he gave Creedon his quietus
with a straight left drive fair in toe teeth.
♦ » » •
The Yankees have not improved the manners or
morals of Australian boxers. Fitzsimmons, an exemplary
husband and father in Australia, has come to grief in the
domestic circle ; Jim Hall is settling down into a saloon
brawler ; Joe Goddard came home with his phenomenal
constitution wrecked ; and if the Yankee papers are to be
believed Dan Creedon, formerly one of the most modest
of boxers, before his Fitzsimmons beating had blossomed
into a “skiter” of the first water. Griffo was beyond
spoiling before he went over. Peter Jackson and George
Dawson appear to be the only two who have preserved
their Australian reputation.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
A Maoriland correspondent writes: “Harry
Laing, the Maori pug., killed whilst attempting to alight
from a moving train some time ago, was the makings of a
splendid fighter. When he fought Paddy Slavin at Wan-
ganui scores of men with no front teeth (the result of
Laing’s playfulness on various occasions) were whooping
for slavin to kill the Maori outright ; but, weak from ill-
ness as Laing was, he held the great P.S. in check for a
considerable time ; and, had he been decently fit, the
Australian would never have progressed further toward
the championship.”

“Victor” writes : During the ensuing A. J.O. and
Tatt.’s meetings I would strongly advise speculators to
keep their eyes on M.L.A. and Georgie, who will both be
returned winners. The latter is a very “fast young
lady,” and experienced very hard luck in being beaten at
Rosehill, a fortnight ago.
The acceptances for the big Xmas handicaps are
very fair, and some good fields should be out. The
Summer Cup will bring about 20 to the post, and of my
original six selections I am now left with Tridentate,
The Captain, CElus, and Donation. They are all doing
well on the training track, and for the absolute winner
I shall look to The Captain, with Tridentate as his most
dangerous opponent.
Moorefield, Marvel and Milkmaid (the latter in
the absence of All There) I look on as a very likely trio
for the Carrington Stakes. The first-named is very well
and has made great improvement since he ran second for
this same race last year ; he will win if he is served with
any luck, and I expect to see Marvel run a great horse.
Hirondelle, I know, is fancied by her party, but, flyer
though she is, she cannot give Moorefield 71bs.
I have four left to represent me in Tatt.’s Cup,
viz. : Loyalty, Donation, Tridentate and Warminster.
Should Tridentate escape a penalty he would win, but if
he lands the Summer Cup 1 then expect to see War-
minster and Loyalty fighting it out at the finish.
The Imperial Arcade, which runs from
Pitt to Castlereagh-street, Sydney, midway be-
tween King and Market-street, is one of the most
complete examples of modern architecture in the
city. Completed in 1891 at large cost, its com-
modious arrangements, ample spaces, and hand-
some decorations made it at once a favorite pub-
lic resort. Everything that skill could devise was
done to render it a permanent attraction to visitors.
The provisions for lighting and sanitation, for
fire-prevention and extinction, and for the com-
fort of tenants and the public, are unsurpassed
anywhere. One of the best hotels in Sydney
■ > jcupies the Castlereagh-street front; and the
r mainder is divided into shops and offices which,
in point of situation and convenience, offer almost
unique advantages.
Sydney Wool Sale.
M'Burnby, Wallis and Co. report.—On Monday,
I )th inst., we held a very successful wool sale, the
wretchedly low state of the market considered. Sound
light and free greasy fleece met with competition which
■ilmost reminded the observer of the days when wool
brought reasonable prices, but, unfortunately, buyers
were only eager up to their limits, and consequently late
rates were not actually exceeded. Inferior greasy
fleece, pieces, and bellies still remain at a very low
I vel as compared with a year ago. Scoured wool
shows no inclination towards an increase in value.
We obtained some remarkably high prices for greasy
lambs' wool, which was purchased on American account
and reached up to 7d. per pound. A few prominent clips,
which passed under the hammer at our sale, are the fol-
lowing:—tiM/ADEihONG, from Addons', at6d. per pound;
JO/DILLADERRY, from Temora, at 5Jd. ; 4S/COOLAC,
very carefully got up, at 6Jd. ; AVELEY/JSM, a strong,
light, free wool, from Young, at 6id. for fleece, and sid.
for pieces, and 7d. for lambs ; JSM, in diamond, s|d. for
fleece and 7d. for lambs; WN/Y/New England, a useful
free wool, from Walcha, at 6Jd. for super, comb, and 6d.
for comb. For 51 bales of scoured oddments, very credit-
ably got up by Messrs. Te Kloot and Whan, of Warren, and
branded WC/A, we obtained 7Jd. for pieces, s£d. for S.
pieces, 6Jd. for bellies, for locks, 7Jd. for pieces and
bellies. Our next sale is on Tuesday, 18th inst., when we
will bring forward another attractive catalogue.

“The Anglican Church is as purely English
as if Heaven were only a remote British colony.
It is as inveterabely national as if all the apostles
had been born in London in the Sixteenth Cen-
tury.”
Ed. Bulletin, —N.S.W, G.P.O. allows
mailboys 14s. a week for forage. N.S.W. mili-
tary authorities allow their officers .£6O a year for
the same purpose for each horse, besides rent of
stable. Why this difference of about 11s. per
week? The mailboy’s horse has much the same
appetite as the officer’s, and is worked a good deal
harder.— Gum.
N.S.W. Pharmacy Bill threatens to intro-
duce a new class of trade-unionists. By all means
let the trade in drugs, and the dispensing of medi-
cine, be regulated in the public interest, so that
the business shall be in the hands of competent
persons ; but let the regulation be done by the
State, and, not by any trade-union whatever. It
would be as reasonable to leave the licensing of
public-houses to the L.V. Association.
“ On every ground,” screams Melbourne
Argus, “the country party has a right to put
in its protest (against land taxation) and to
declare for a Budget of economy, of equity, and
of hope.”
r i it’s the sort of thing which should be sung in
■ Ainas pantomimes, instead of bunkum about
lu • goodness of the Royal Family.
Robert Reid, the Victorian softgoods im-
porter —who, being an importer, is naturally also a
Freetrader —points out, as one of the dreadful
results of Protection, that many people have taki n
to drinking Joshua’s brandy—a local production
—instead of using the imported article; and the
result is a serious loss of revenue. Joshua’s
brandy has been declared by European experts to
be of first-class quality ; also, it is much cheaper
than the foreign article; therefore, the only objec-
tion to it is that it is a Victorian product which
supplies employment and wages to Victori; ns
instead of to Europeans. The mental state of the
individual who wants to put down local industiies,
merely because they reduceimports, must be some-
thing unique. By-and-bye it will dawn on Reid
that Australian sheep require to be abolished
because they stop the importation of wool.
The present N.S.W, Assembly, which is
the first one elected under manhood suffrage, is
much more Democratic than the one which pre-
ceded it, yet the direct Labor-party is much
smaller and distinctly less influential than in last
Parliament. The inference is that One-man-one-
vote doesn’t tend to create a Labor-party, which
simply means class representation for the horny-
handed section of the community only ; but rather
to build up a Democratic-party, which represents
the progressive sentiment of the nation at large.
Purely class representation, whether it be for
shearers, lawyers, or importers, is an evil, and
manhood suffrage promises, in time, to make an
end of it.
We are often told by merchants, &c., of
the danger of interfering in the interests of work-
men with the sacred principle of freedom of con-
tract. The Yic. Sale of Goods Bill, which simply
codifies the existing law, provides that a part of a
contract, which the parties declare to be a war-
ranty, i.e., not indispensable, may nevertheless
be held to be indispensable by the Court. But
this is only in the interests of merchants, and
what applies to them can have no analogy with
the case of the common laborer.
Dear Bulletin, —It was refreshing to read
your remarks concerning the “private wealth”
gag, which has been hurled at Australians until
they have got into the habit of thinking them-
selves the richest people on earth. The private
command of ready cash in this country is very
limited. Men have no loose sovereigns lying
about in writing-desks. Wives, as a rule, can’t
pay an unexpected bill at first sight. Rents are
collected weekly ; the gas account comes in once a
month, instead of once a quarter. Money is scarce
among the many, and millionaires are few.—F.
Dear Bulletin, Grant Allen’s new
hedonism is only the same old floundering round
the gratuitous assumptions that the universe
pivots on man, and that what he calls “ wrong ”
isn’t as essential a part of the scheme of things as
what he calls “right.” There is absolutely
nothing to show that “race-elevation” is any
more normal than “race-degradation,” or that the
perpetuation of the human is of any greater cos-
mic importance than the perpetuation of the
oyster. “Evil” —from the man-fly’s point of
vi ew —ig whatever tends to brush him off the
wheel of Nature. But the wheel doesn’t care.
—Heretic.
The Blue Mountain hotel at Toowoomba,
Q., 3000 feet above the sea, is an ideal place for
business and care-worried humanity to seek holi-
day solace in. The climate and surroundings are
wholly delightful.
Melb. Sun advertises an amateur photo-
graphers’ competition which is worth looking into,
the prizes totalling 20 guineas. The want of
classification as to size and finish, however, is a
drawback that should be put straight at once.
W. H. Glen & Co., the well-known Melb.
music publishers, have just issued three Christmas
Annuals containing a fine assortment of songs,
dance pieces, &c. They comprise Nos. 33 and 34
of “The Federal Music Books,” each include g
30 pages of full size sheet music, with recitations
and capital portraits of J. L. Toole and Kate
Vaughan ; and No. 28 of Glen’s Musical Cabinet,
which contains, “Because I love thee so,” by J.
A. Robertson ; “ The blind girl to her harp; ”
“She wore a wreath of roses;” “Where’er you
walk;” and a number of new songs sure to
become popular.
When yearly deficits are swelling
The size of our national debt,
Instead of persistently dwelling
On payments that have to be met,
A Treasurer ought to start washing
His hands with invisible soap.
And rise in his place, with a jubilant face.
To give us a Budget of Hope—
’Ope, ’ope—
A nice little Budget of Hope.
A truce to this taxing and croaking,
And give the “ poor farmer ” a chance ;
Depression’s a matter for joking—
Let’s call on the Chair for a dance.
We want a gay johnnie like Carter :
Witli trouble he’s able to cope,
Though ids figures were wrong, as we knew all along,
He gave us a Budget of Hope—
’Ope, ’ope—
A nice little Budget of Hope.
Hooroo for the banks and the squatters
Who work for the good of the land,
These Radical members are “ rotters
A beastly, burglarious band.
And, as for this dangerous Turner,
Why cannot lie chuck it, and “slope,”
if he doesn't intend to behave like a friend
And give us a Budget of Hope—
’Ope, ’ope—
And trive us a Budget of Hope.

The “SUN”
AMATEUR PHOTOGRAPHIC
OOMFBTITIOar.
The Proprietor of the Sun has pleasure in announcing a
Photographic Competition for Amateurs throughout the
Australian colonies under the following conditions:
All competitors must be amateurs, and all work sub-
mitted for competition must be the bona fide work of the
competitor througl out.
For the purposes of this competition, an amateur will
be considered as one who does not gain his or her liveli-
hood by photography.
All photos, are to be addressed to the Editor, the Sun,
Post Office Place, Melbourne, Victoria.
All pictures forwarded for competition will become the
property of the Editor, who will reserve the right to re-
publish the same in the pages of the Sun from time to
time. In no case will any pictures be returned.
Competitors are at liberty to forward as many pictures
aa they wish for competition.
It is optional whether the work submitted be mounted
or not.
Amateur competitors for the Stiff prizes must not sub-
mit any work previously shown at any exhibition or
competition. All pictures must be taken specially for
the Sun.
A nom de plume only must be written on the back of
each print. The nom de plume used, with name and
address written in full, must be sent in a sealed envelope.
Work sent with insufficient postage will be rejected.
In order that photographers may ha\e the benefit of
the holidays, the competition will remain open until the
21st January, 1895.
The choice of subject for the work is left entirely with
competitors.
First Prize - Cash £lO 10s
Second ~ - value £7 7s
(First-class Modern Outfit of J-plate Camera and
Lens, presented by Messrs. Baker & Rouse)
Third „ - „ £3 3s
(Detective Hand Camera)
The best specimens of work submitted for competition
will be re-produced in the Sun half tone process.

THIS magnificent Hotel, situated on the sum-
mit of the Main Range—3oooft. above the

IN pursuance of the provisions of the “ Inland
Waters Fisheries Act of 1887,” I, Sir Robert
William Dulf, the Governor of the Colony of New
South Wales, with the advice of the Executive
Council, do, by this ray proclamation, declare that
the portion of the Oudgegong River at Mudgee
specified in the schedule hereto appended shall be
closed against the use of fishing-nets for a period
of two years from the date hereof.
Given under my hand and seal, at Government
House, Sydney, this twenty-ninth day of
JSiovember, in the year of our Lord one thou-
sand eisrht hundred and ninety-four, and in
the fifty-eighth year of Her Majesty’s reign.
By His Excellency’s command.
JAMES N. BRUNKER.
GOD SAVE THE QUEEN!
SCHEDULE.
Counties of Phillip and Wellington. The whole
of that portion of the Oudgegong River fronting
the town of Mudgee, including all affluents and
tributaries ; bounded on the north-west by Wil-
bertree Bridge, distant about two chains from
Pipeclay Creek ; and on the south-west by the
eastern boundary of the Melrose estate, being J.
W. Lawson’s portion No. 54 of 960 acres. [9256]

APPLICATION FOR LEASES FOR OYSTER CULTURE.
It is hereby notified for general information that the undermentioned persons have applied to lease
for Oyster Culture the portion of land set opposite their names in the annexed schedule.
Tracings showing the position of the portion enumerated may be inspected at this Department
daily, excepting Saturdays, between 11 and 3 o’clock, and on Saturdays between 11 and 12 o’clock.
Any person may, by memorial to the Commissioners of Fisheries, within thirty days from the date
of this notice, and on grounds to be stated in such memorial, pray that a lease of the portion may not
be granted.
LINDSAY THOMPSON,
Chief Inspector of Fisheries.

OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
In the matter of the application of The Perpetual Trustee Company, Limited, mortgagees under
memorandum of mortgage from Ada Beatrice Brown (wife of John James Forrester Brown), No.
159,249,dated December 11, 1889, over the whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol.
905, folio 19.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until January 25, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under
her, unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy
the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and
interest up to the time of such sale.
A Plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Eliza-
beth-street, Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 14th day of December, 1894, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
CHARLES PINHEY. Registrar-General.

107,584, dated May 3, 1886, over the whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 905,
folio 66.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts,
as required by section 113 of the Real Property Act : Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until the 25th January, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under him,
unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy the prin-
cipal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest
up to the time of such sale.
A plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Elizabeth-
street, Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 14th day of December, 1894, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.

The smashed portion of the Melb. Falls
Bridge, to which attention was drawn in The
Bulletin two years back, has remained un-
repaired for four years past, or ever since the
accident occurred. There is simply a row of hurdles
chained round the opening to keep drunks from
falling in. Melbourne is hard-up.
The Melb. Society for the Assistance ot
Persons of Education reports, with a glow, having
done noble work. This society differs from the
cold-blooded charity-organisation gang, inasmuch
as where the latter “inquires” the former “ex-
amines.” The S. for A. of P. of E., when asked
for a soup-ticket or a pair of cast-off pants,
requires the applicant to translate a slab of
Horace and do a compound proportion sum in
decimals.
You could not sell a tomahawk or axe with
a gold-painted head, no matter how good or cheap,
in any of the New Hebrides group. Black-painted
heads are the only saleable sort. Once a German
dealer did a great business trip with the gilded
sort, selling cheap, of course ; but after the axes
had been in use a day or two the steel tipping fell
off and left a useless soft-metal tool. Had this
dealer not got away quickly, he would have had
the real article (thrown from somewhere in the
rear with the deadly overarm whirl) in his skull.
Droving-story by M , a well-known
Queenslander : “I was coming down with a mob
of 1500 cattle, in a big drought, and got into a
fearfully dry country. The beasts hadn’t had a
drink for five days, and I was riding ahead won-
dering what was going to be the end of it all when
I came upon a huge bottle-tree, at which I pulled
up, for it struck me that there was enough water
in that tree to save my mob if I could only get at
it. I went to camp, got an auger and came back
with two men and we rigged up troughs and
tapped the tree and watered the whole 1500, with
lots to spare. You fellows mightn’t believe it,
but Sam there can tell you it’s true.” “True as
Gospel,” said Sam, “ for I came past there six
weeks afterwards and caught a cod-fish.”
A (let us say Queensland) “financier”
combined business with pleasure the other day.
(Jailed on a storekeeper, who was “ behind,” and
graciously informed him that he intended writting
him when he got back to town. Next day the
usurer hired a boat and fisherman for a few hours,
for a price which was to include all the fish cap-
tured. The financier probably did not know that
the fisherman was the debtor’s son-in-law, but it is
quite certain that when the boat was on the fish-
ing-ground the fisherman unfortunately opened
the boat’s bung-hole, lost the peg overboard (so he
said), and, whilst sitting on the orifice to keep
the water out, asked Abraham what he would
give for the life-belt which he (the fisherman) had
under his jersey. There being a corner in life-
belts just then, and bung-hole-stoppers having
risen to a fabulous price, a sum—in notes —passed,
which enabled the fisherman to find another peg
to stop the leak and to put by a tidy sum, after
“ landing ” Abraham in both ways, and taking
up the overdue bill of his father-in-law.

Dear Bulletin, — While you are on the
subject of “Colonial ” slang or colloquialisms, why
not get your correspondents to supply original
expressions with, as far as possible, derivations?
I3y-and-bye many words that are now slang will
drift into colloquialisms, to be later assimilated
by the great Australian language. If we are going
to be, a nation we are going to develope a lan-
guage, and it’s never too soon to commence col-
lecting philological data which to-day are new
and to-morrow are forgotten. Some examples :
“Hung up”: Mining expression derived from practice
of hanging-up the stampers of a battery when idle.
Meaning, work suspended.
“Larrikin”: From thieves' patter, lerrya, thief; kin, young
—this despite the plausible Sergeant Dalton yarn.
“ Leary cove ” is one who knows all about the
business of stealing and disposing of stolen goods.
“Gone bung”: Financially dead—from aboriginal
“ bong,” dead—as in bilfabong, literally dead-water,
applied to back-water.
“ Gibba ” : A stone (aboriginal).
“Rye-buck”: “All right,” “ It’s a bargain "—from
Hebrew “ reivoch,” a bargain.
“Bart" [ Sydney larrikinese. Derivation?
The future language of Australia will have a strong
tincture of “Yiddish,” from the freedom with
which the argot is used by the bookie crowd and
absorbed and distributed by the stable and race-
course habitues, say 9-10ths of the population.—
WurrY M.

DO THOU LIKEWISE.
When one sets a candle in a window on a dark
night he never can tell how many lost and be-
wildered travellers it may guide on their way.
I once knew a dear lady who habitually did it
on every dark night. The road that passed her
house was always forsaken and lonesome enough,
and houses were far apart. So, on the general
principle of goodwill to men, she placed her
candle in a sort of projecting window, where it
would shine both ways and do the most good.
She is dead and gone now, but let us hope that
her family keeps up the custom. To be sure, it
brought her many a strange guest, yet she did
what she could for them, and never grumbled—
pay or no pay.
A year or two ago a little book was printed,
containing an account, by Mr. John Hodson, of
Warboys, Hunts, of the way he was cured of a
wasting disease _ by Mother Seigel’s Curative
byrup—a complaint contracted in India, where
Mr. Hodson was once a soldier in the British
Army.
This candle of Hodson’s beamed hopefully in
all directions, and, among others, its rays fell
upon the eyes of a woman who needed light at
that particular time. How it came to pass she
tells m a letter, from which we quote the follow-
ing :
“ I was,” she says, “always healthy up to
May, 1884, when I had an attack of gastric
fever, which left me low and feeble. I had a
bad taste in the mouth, and after everything I
ate I had great pain in the chest and sides, and
also dreadful pain at the back and between the
shoulder-blades, and a sinking feeling at the pit
of the stomach. My legs trembled and shook
under me, so I could not walk out. Indeed, it
was as much as I could do to get across the
floor. A dry, hacking cough set in and shook
me very much, and I lost a deal of sleep.
“ As I grew weaker I was confined to my bed
more and more, and my daughter was obliged to
take my place in doing the housework. A doctor
attended me for over a year. He tried first one
kind of medicine and then another, but none of
them helped me. At the end of the year the
doctor said he could do no more for me, and
recommended me, as a last resort, to try a change
of air.
“I had got a despair of ever getting better
when a book was sent to me full of statements
from different people, telling how they had been
made well of various complaints by using Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and I read of a young
man living at Warboys, near me, having been
cured in a marvellous manner by this medicine.
I got a bottle from Messrs. Palmer and Sons,
chemists, Ramsey, and, after I had taken it a
week, I felt relief and gained strength. All my
vain left me, and I have never looked behind me
since. I take an occasional dose of the Syrup
and keep in excellent health. I give you full
permission to use this letter as you think fit.
Yours truly, (signed) Sarah Mason, Ramsey
Heights, Ramsey, Hunts, January 27th, 1892.”
What better use could we possibly put this
lady’s letter to than to publish it ? In this way
it has fallen into the present reader’s hands, and
may turn out to be a candle in the window to
show him refuge and shelter beside some long and
gloomy path. A word more on this very point.
It is not a matter of choice whether we are to
help one another. We are obliged and bound to do
so by every consideration of morality and utility.
Humanity is like the body of one man : if a single
part is neglected, the rest must pay the penalty.
Therefore, set candles in windows. If, for
instance, Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup has
done you good, say so, publish it, make it known.
Others near you may be groping in darkness.
And it is all the more a subject of rejoicing that
this remedy, as in the above case, conquers indi-
gestion and dyspepsia, because that complaint it
is which clouds the sun for millions on Life’s
hard road.

A Bundaberg (Q.) farmer recently took a
rope and started into the bush leading his aged
father along with him. When he reached the
f big tree he climbed to a top branch, uttered a
shrill whoop or two, and hanged himself. Mean-
while, the poor father, who was too old for tree-
climbing, was alternately delivering impassioned
addresses to the suicide, and clawing with his
withered hands at the trunk in vain efforts to
ascend.
Dear Bulletin, —Yet another tight place.
J riend of mine, in white suit, while crossing pad-
dock near homestead on scorching hut day, was
chased by a bull. He shinned up a tree, mean-
ing to stay until the brute went away, or some of
the hands came in sight. Bull showed no inclina-
tion to leave, and the climber began to get
cramped. A hive of bees had swarmed at home-
stead, and were making for his tree, his white
apparel inviting them to settle on him. They
buzzed round him and (he couldn’t move one way
or the other) he tried to “shoo” them off, but
they only stung him on the face the more. His
ones for help excited the bull, who bellowed his
loudest, and he was just thinking whether it would
not, be better to slide down and trust to the mercy
of the beast when help came.—S.
When the wrecked Wairarapa lurched over
on her side a, passenger named Chamberlain—
according to his evidence afterwards given at the
inquest—was crawling forward with some others
on the outside of the ship. “ By-and-bye an old
friend of mine, William Whaley, came along.
When I saw him I said ‘ Hulloa, Whaley !’ He
replied ‘Hulloa, old man; it’s ago with us this
time!’ ‘Oh, I don’t know, old chap,’l said:
keep your pecker up. You never know till the
numbers are up. ’ Shortly after that a big wave
came along, and the pessimist was taken, while
the optimist was left. Probably the optimist, by
reason of his creed, hung on the tighter of the
two.

Dear Bulletin, here’s an enquiry
Which puzzles me quite:
Why poets of spirit so fiery,
Who both live and write,
Whose mind is a web of fine tissue—
Men like Gordon and Boake (both, we miss you!),
“ The Banjo,” whose note still may issue
Our sense to delight—
Persist beyond measure in sighing.
Why brood in such sadness ?
No-one for a moment’s denying
That life has its badness;
But wail not, ye gifted, who can sing,
In long lamentations enhancing
Life’s manifold woes, without glancing
At all its great gladness.
Such plaints give but half of our life-song,
The whole why not tell ?
You know the bright tabor-and-fife-song,
Nb less than the knell.
Let the songs of our land be as cheerful
As life is : our sons are not fearful;
In ill-luck too brave to be tearful:
Hymn that side as well!
E.R.G.
[But “ Banjo ” doesn’t sigh.— Ed. B.]

The Ballad of Stuttering
Tim,
(ILLUSTRATING THE SURVIVAL OF THE FITTEST.)
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
This is the yarn of Stuttering Jim, the girl, and
the other man,
The strangest yarn that ever was told since ever
the world began;
The yarn that was told by Stuttering Jim when
the hour of the dawn was nigh,
And the fire grew dim, and the pipes went out,
and the whisky-bottle was dry.
There are tales that are told in the darkness, and
tales that are told in the light,
And some are fit for the morning star, and some
for the secret night;
But the tale that he told is the tale to tell when
the camp-fire’s wan and grey,
And the dawn breaks into the house of night with
the blood-red hammers of day.
Now, Jim was a man of thirty-two, though he
looked a good three-score,
For his back was bent, and his eyes were red, and
the hue of his hair was hoar.
There was me, and Pell, and Carroty Joe, and
Barney and Bandy Gray,
But never a word came out of the crowd as he
gave himself away.
And Jim he talked as he mostly did when the
whisky slipped his tongue,
Of the time when his back was straight and
strong, and his looks, like his years, were
young,
And he whined of a day when his tongue could
wag as fast as a man would think,
When his eyes were bright, and all was well,
before he took to the drink.
And he talked of a girl that lived hard by to a
place that he had of his own,
With a face like the flower that glows in the bush
when the winter is one-half flown.
She could ride all day, and dance all night, and
be up with the morning dew,
And milk the cows on her father’s farm, as a
well-bred girl should do.
And though she loved dancing and dainty things,
as a healthy maiden must,
There was never a man of the sons of men could
have brought her head to the dust;
But she and Jim got talking of love, which is
dangerous talk for a man,
And the end of it was they plighted their troth —
and then the trouble began.
For there came a chap to her father’s farm, a bit
of a ne’er-do-well,
Who had gone the pace for all it was worth till
they shipped him away to hell.
And he got struck on this girl of Jim’s till his
blood was all of a flame,
And he pushed old Jim for the foremost place like
a man that was used to the game.
And Jim got riled, and chopped about, for he saw
how the current blew,
And now he was mad, and now he was gay, and
he kept the girl in a stew.
But the girl stuck fast to her plighted troth, and
treated it all as a jest—
She clung as the steel wedge clings to the wood,
but she liked the other chap best.
And Jim he told how the girl’s old man, and her
mother, and all the three
Went up one day to the Forty-mile Bush on a
sort of a picnic spree.
And Jim and the girl got wandering off, as a man
and. a maid will do,
And the place was supple-jack up to the eyes, and
as flat as a cake all through.
And there’s some that go and twist and turn and
come out safe and sound,
And there’s some that gt>, and never come back,
for they leave their bones on the ground.
And there’s dandy bushes this side of hell for a
man that is sick of the play,
But the dandiest bush in all that land is the bush
that they found that day.
Then the old man up and off to the farm, for he
said that he trusted Jim
To bring his girl by another road home, for noth-
ing would happen to him.
But the English chap hung a bit behind, and he
crammed his pockets with food,
And the very first chance the beggar saw he was
off and away for the wood.
Then Jim he told how he and the girl had tangled
the bush about,
And how, by dark, the other man came to show
them a pathway out;
But what should a Londoner know of the bush
more than they who are native-born ?
He led them the way that leads to death betwixt
the night and the morn.
For the bush was as thick as a paddock of maize,
and sharp as a tiger’s claw,
With the supple-jack vines, and the saws and
spines of toi and tatara-moa.
But still he thought they’d a kind of show if only
the food would last,
And he parted some bread ’twixt Jim and the girl,
but he shut his own mouth for a fast.
And another day went, and another day came,
and a day and a night thereto,
And they came to a bit of a rise at last where a
peep of the sky got through,
And they lighted a fire of leaves and logs, and
settled them down to wait
Till the rescue came. And the days and nights
went past till they numbered eight.
Their food was done on the sixth day out, though
they rationed it down to a crumb,
And their water was most of it slime from the
swamp, and the flesh of the taraire plum ; _
And the suns that rose and the suns that set
looked down in their giddy whirl
On the English beggar that kept his fast for the
love of a starving girl.
And he and the girl were weak as rats, and
twisted with deadly pain,
And pale as the tea-tree flower that blows at the
end of the winter rain.
But Jim hung out from day to day, with a face
of steady cheer,
Till the Englishman woke from a bit of a doze,
and he reckoned the thing looked queer.
So he followed Jim down to the edge of the
swamp, though the beggar could barely stand,
And he spied him there, like a rat in a hole, with
a crust of bread in his hand.
Eor Jim had found the stuff in the pouch ere the
sixth dawn grew to day,
And he thought of his love, and he thought of his
faith, but life seemed better than they.
For life is life, and death is death, and each man
“ goes alone,”
And death is a frozen No Man’s Land, and life is
a torrid zone;
And the way of the world is a curious way, and
curious creatures thrive,
And the false man lives and the true man dies,
and only the fit survive.
Then the English chap stepped out of his lair, and
they stood by the swamp alone,
And he saw Jim bury the crust in the fern, as a
dog will bury a bone,
And he cried : “I have given my life for yours, if
ever this world goes round,
But I’ll win her love for a dying man in lieu of a
living hound.
“ I have tortured my flesh for twelve long days to
give her the joy of life,
But now she is mine for the rest of time, and the
devil may find you a wife.
I have gambled and sworn and lied and mocked
and humbled myself to none,
But I’m more of a man with all my sins than
you with your single one.
“ I have drunken and danced and had my fling,
and laughed at the “ Promise of May,’’
But not for all that God has to give would I stand
in your shoes to-day.”
And he up and off where the young girl sat, with
her eyes fixed hard on the sod,
And he said ; “It is better to know to-day than
to learn to-morrow from God.
“ You’ve set your heart on the form of a man, but
he’d never the soul of a hound,
And it’s better to die with the truth all told than
go with a lie to the ground.”
And he told the tale with a fainting voice, and a
face like a winding-sheet,
And bade her God-speed, and clutched his throat,
and dropped down dead at her feet.
Then Jim he told how he slunk from the swamp,
for he dreaded to die alone,
And he saw the girl where she sat by her dead,
with her face set still as a stone ;
And the girl looked up from the eyes of the corpse
with their blank and ghastly stare,
And she looked at the live and she looked at the
dead, and the dead face seemed more fair.
And she smiled and sighed, and put out her arms
and gathered the corpse to her breast,
And she said : “ You have had my plighted troth,
but I loved the dead man best.
And I fear he played us false with the food, or
how should it come to pass
That a strong man, Tull of the lust of life, should
die in the arms of a lass ?
“You’ll come safe out from the gates of death
while the fiend has work to do,
And you’ll find a mate in the warm, full world,
but I’m not the girl for you.
The love you gave was a dainty thing as the hue
of a sunset sky.
But the love of the dead was the love of a man,
for it taught him the way to die.”
And the day went down and the day came up, and
a long cold night thereto,
And then the rescue broke through the woods, as
rescues mostly do.
For twelve long days they had followed the trail
where hardly a bird could dive,
And they carried two corpses out of the bush, but
Jim they carried alive.
There are tales that are told in the darkness, and
tales that are told in the light,
And some are fit for the morning star, and some
for the secret night;
But the tale Jim told is the tale to tell when the
camp fire’s wan and grey,
And the dawn breaks into the house of night with
the blood-red hammers of day.
Maoriland.

A Sydney tobacco firm offered an M.L.A.
£5 to introduce into an address in the Legislative
Assembly the name of a particular brand of weed.
Which proves that the enterprising party doesn’t
know everything, as it is certain that, if such an
allusion were made, the name of the brand would
be excluded from the press reports. The incident,
however, suggests one danger against which a
popularised daily Hansard would need to guard.

Twenty-five Lashes.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I didn’t care to drink alone, so asked the young
fellow to join me.
He was out of work, he said ; out of gaol also —
the latter, apparently, a new experience.
“F’r, y’see it’s this way, boss, w’en the Weedin’
cops gets ter know a bloke, ’n ’ave ’im through
their ’ands wunst er twice, they alwus pick ’im up
wenever they want ter make a case, ’n as ’e’s
alwus ‘well known,’ ’e’s bound ter go inter the
nick. Carn’t live ’onest if ’e wanted ter.
“ I ain’t goin’ in no more, not if I can ’elp it.
“ Last I gob was two stretch for toppin’ a bloke
orf wid an ’arf-a-brick, ’n bootin’ ’im round a bib.
I did it right enough, too—’n I’d do it agen if ’e
served me the same way, if I ’ad to get a nother
twenty-five.”
“ What? were you flogged?”
“ Wasn’t I! Put yer ’and up ’ere under me
bloomin’ coat ’n y’ll feel,
“Yes. I took five-’n-twenty of ’em like a toff —
but never no more —not me.”
“Hurt?”
“ Did y’ ever get a cub wid a w’ip when y’ was
a kid ?”
“Yes.”
“ Madey’ jump? ”
“ Rather.”
“ Well, w’en y’r tied up f’r a floggin’ y’ carn’t
jump. See? Well, if y’ can fancy nine w’ips
cornin’ down at wunst, ’n’ fancy wantin’ ter jump,
’n’ carn’b, y’ get a kinda idea.
“ W’en I was took out f’r my bit o’ lacin’ I felt
jes’ like I felt wunst w’en I got a punch over the
’art in a boxin’ room. I’ve ’erd of a bloke’s ’art
goin’ inter ’is Weedin’ boots, but, by , I thorb
myen ’ad gone out through the bloomin’ lace ’oles.
“ I was tied up by a bull of a bloke, ’oo strapped
me ’ands so ’igh me feet was jes’ tippin’ the
ground. Then ’e straps me arms, ’n thighs, ’n
knees, 'n ankles, ’n gives me me shirt to chew
between the strokes.
“ Then ’e sheds ’is coat, ’n claws ’is set o’ boot-
laces, ’n then ’e swings em round ’n down they
comes.
“I thorb ’e’d ’it me in the belly. I didn’t feel
the pain on me back, at the first one, but in me
stomach, ’n there I felt ’s if all me inside was
cornin’ up inter me neck.
“ It seemed’s if it was a arf a nour before I got
the next one, ’n by then me bloomin’ back begun
to tingle, ’n I felt like’s if fifty devils was jabbin’
me with five million red-’ot needles. Then I ’erd
the swish of the lash ’n got the second. I knew
jes’ w’ere I was ’it that time, ’n I chewed a chunk
outer me shirt so’s I wouldn’t yell.
“ There was a mob o’ people there—reporters, I
s’pose, ’n I didn’t want to squeak. None o’ them
coves can ever write about a floggin’ till they’ve
’ad one, ’n ’ere’s a cove ’ud like to give ’em a taste
o’ pussy’s tail all round f’r nixey. Wot do they
wanter come ’n see a poor devil chopped up f’r?
“ Funny thing, ain’t it? I never felt any more
after that in the way o’ pain till I was finished
wid. Not till I’d bin in the orsepital f’r a nour er
so. Then I was put through all right. Couldn’t
sleep n’r eat—only drink all that night, ’n’ nex’
day couldn’t ’ardly move, ’n’ wen the cuts was
’eelin’ up I felt like me skin was too tight f’r me
back, ’n’ I waa lightened ter stoop down f’r fear
I’d bust it.
“ By-’m’-by X got all right, n’ now I don’t teel
nothin*. Never have to scratch my back though,
now.”
“Why?”
“ Couldn’t feel nothin’ there—it’s like leather.
“ I don’t want ter go back ter the nick, but if I
ever get a charnce ter ’ave a crack at that bloke
wot flailed me, by the livin’ I’ll crack ’is skull
like a rotten goose egg—the dog.
“ I stoushed the bloke w’en I was in a tearin’
temper—bub ’e goes ’n chops chaps up in cold
blood. ’E’s a bigger brute than the coves ’e
cuts up.
“ Nex’ time I wanter top a joker off I’ll lay f’r
’im till ’e’s by ’is bleedin’ self.
“ No more twenty-fives f’r me.
“ There’s more days than one ter deal wid a
bloke wot narks y’.”
N.S.W. Patents office was lately visited
by a genius who wanted a patent for a poem—
“me own manufacture,” he said, nodding his
head triumphantly. “ In fact, it’s more’n a poem,
it’s a song ” —and he proceeded to sing it to the
tune of “the Animals out of the Ark.” Up to
date he hasn’t got his patent.

Historian Gibbon’s centenary was cele-
brated in London, Nov. 15. The idea of remem-
bering the man who formulated the most crushing
indictment ever written against the saints and
martyrs of primitive Christianity would have been
regarded with horror in England only 50 years
ago ; and the fact that nob even the religious press
has now a stone to throw at Gibbon’s memory is
another proof of the disrepute into which theo-
logical dogma has fallen since his time.
Dear Bulletin, —Another “tight place.”
Just before the boom on Hill End, I was doing a
night-shift in the Western Undaunted. She was
down some 300 feet, working with a whim; and
centred within 20 or 30 feet of the surface, sinking
straight to out the famous golden veins of Brown’s,
Porter’s, and Hurley’s. I had for a mate Harry
O (father of a well-known bookie, who, by the
by, was working in the same shaft). We fired a
couple of holes and had crib. When I started to
go below, it was blowing and raining so hard that
you couldn’t keep a candle alight—so, standing on
the edge of the heavy iron bucket, I gave the
word, “Lower away below the centreing and stop”
—so that I could get a light. Down, down in the
darkness; a sudden bump, and I am jammed
against the southern end of the shaft. The
bucket has caught the centreing. Down goes the
rope past me, its weight increasing the jam. A
thousand thoughts flash through ray brain in an
instant. The weight of the rope must drag the
bucket down; I determine to hold on to the rope if
the bucket goes—bub what a smash if I miss!
The rope stops ; I shout, “ Heave up ! heave up !”
The rope quickly ascends; lam in dread that it
may unship the bucket, so prepare for a desperate
grip. With the rattle of the shackle and chain
attached to the bucket —how Ido grip ! “ What’s
up? What’s wrong?” is the question as I arrive
on the edge of the bucket at the landing. “ Oh,
nothing; just caught napping, that’s all. Lower
away right to the bottom this time, Billy.”—
Yours, Jbronq.
Cohen I Cohen! Cohn!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In 1861 Edward Stringer discovered gold at (Stringer’s
Creek) the foot of Long Tunnel Hill, Walhalla. The reef
now worked by the various Long Tunnel companies was
afterwards owned by Cohen, a Wulhalla auctioneer and
agent, and is called Cohen’s Reef. Stringer died desti-
tute.
Cohen ! Cohen !
Stringer skimmed the cream at Ballarat, at Fryer’s
and Sandy Stone;
Stringer walked from Woods’ Point east’ard every
blessed inch alone;
Stringer climbed Walhalla’s ranges when the
mountain winds were blowin’,
But when auctioneers and such came Stringer
thought he’d best be goin’—
Cohen! Cohen 1
Cohen! Cohen!
Stringer forced the safe of Nature ; Stringer burst
her bolts and bars ;
Miners work to find the metal and their work the
Cohen mars.
Oh, the mighty clan of Cohen ! To their hands
for ever flowin’
Come the stolen meeds of labor, earned by sweat
—annexed by law,
By the toil-less syndicator of the large insatiate
maw;
To that race which tots the rows up of the other
fellow’s hoein’—
Cohen! Cohen !
Cohen! Cohen !
Stringer walked to wild Walhalla —Cohen drove
there with a coach.
Cohen got the reef, although he later made his
first approach,
Cohen learned to oast up figures—Cohen knew
that he who stays
Office-scheming while the other fellow works shall
end his days
Fat and rich and known, and knowin’, with the
world’s wealth to him flowin’,
He who made that wealth by sweating to a
pauper’s grave is goin’—
Cohen! Cohen!
Cohen ! Cohen !
Fate’s an auctioneer that takes your nod and
passes other bids,
Makes the Gentiles sweat the sweating—makes
the sweaters of the Yids ;
Gives to Stringer burial squalid—gives to Ike the
monument
Made of brass, and Jewry-figured—paid by inter-
est shent per shent.
For while labor wears its brain-pan in the sinews
of the arms,
The scheming drone shall own the earth —its
mining and its farms.
To starvation Labor’s goin’—unto wealth pro-
gresses Cohen,
And it isn’t far from Cohen until Stringer’s really
gone ;
For whatever labor’s doin’, still the coin to
Cohen’s flowin’.
The commission-agent’s mowin’ down the stringers
—flesh and bone,
An’ the syndicators, knowin’, stow the spoil and
don’t stop stowin’
Till the lab’rer’s got past Oohen—till his chance
rests on the “ Cohen ”
For a moment, and is—“ Cohn.”
Melb,
RANDOLPH THE RECKLESS.
A Melb. Mongolian hawker lately bought
lewt. potatoes, and retailed 3cwb., having 171bs.
left. This sort of thing in the army commissariat
has made it such an easy snap for the Jap.
Dear Bulletin, —Re capital punishment.
The other day I found a half-grown diamond spar-
row on our verandah. I restored it to what
seemed its native nest; but must have placed it
amongst a hostile community, as next morning I
found it dead—suspended by a long hair from the
edge of the nest. —H,, Wilcannia, 16th Nov.
The stand taken by Labor-members in Q.
Parliament on the innocent-looking Land Pur-
chase Bill merely amounts to a demand that pur-
chases shall be made subject to the sanction of
Parliament. Ministers declare they will sacrifice
the Bill rather than take such an amendment.
It would be well for Australia if in every Parlia-
ment members made a similar stand to have land
purchases negotiated by Minister, subject to the
“santion of Parliament.”
According to the absentee clause in N.S. W.
Land-tax Bill, “ any owner of land in New South
Wales who shall not have resided within the
colony for a period equal to four years in every
period of six years immediately preceding the
year of assessment shall pay tax on an additional
20 per centum,” &c. Under this clumsily-drafted
clause a man who came to New South Wales three
years ago, and bought land, and settled down to
spend the rest of his life in the province, would
be taxable as an absentee, because he hadn’t been
in the country for four years out of the last six.
The Land-tax Bill was evidently drafted by a
lawyer.
Mining man pulls up very late at country
pub, and demands a bed. “ Haven’t a room to
spare in the house,” replies the landlord ; “show’s
on just now.” “Bub I must sleep somewhere,
and I’m going to.” “ Well, there’s a spare bed in
the billiard-room, but a lady’s in the other there.
Still, if you’ll go in in the dark and get out early
to-morrow morning when the boobs sneaks in to
call you, you can have it. Bub be quiet, and don’t
say a word to anyone.” Manganese assented, and
the position of the couch having been whisperingly
pointed out, he retired to roost. Ten minutes
later he burst into the bar, with his hair on end.
“My God!” he cried to the landlord, “that
woman’s dead!” “Yes,” blandly replied Mr.
Snakejuice; “ but how did you find out ?”

NOTICE.
Readers of The Bulletin, where-
ever situated, will greatly oblige by
informing the Managing Director of
any locality where they may be unable
to procure the paper, and naming
any reliable person who would pro-
bably undertake its sale.

H. PATESON, Manager.
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on hush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-ooluran where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived bv Tub Bulletin.
